Valid Capital in Mudarabah: Currency Requirement
Ruling on capital type for valid mudarabah with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires mudarabah capital to be currency (dirhams, dinars, or current coins) and evaluate the opposing views of Ibn Abi Layla and Malik using the hadith 'no profit from what is not guaranteed'.

Core Idea
Mudarabah capital must be currency because profit partnership requires fungible capital that can be guaranteed by return.

This lesson explores a critical condition for a valid mudarabah: the nature of the capital. While many think any valuable item can be used, the Hanafi school strictly limits capital to gold, silver, or current coins. The passage presents the Hanafi position, the reasoning, and the counterarguments from other schools, together with the decisive proof from the Prophet's (ﷺ) prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi condition
Mudarabah is only valid with capital in which partnership (shirkah) is valid. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this means dirhams or dinars; Muhammad adds current coins (fulus ra'ij).
Other items like foodstuffs or commodities are not acceptable.
2
Understand the reason (sirr)
Mudarabah is a partnership in profit. Partnership in profit requires the capital to be fungible (mithli) so that it can be returned in kind. Currency is mithli; other items are 'ayn (specific) and cannot be returned identically.
The 'secrecy' here refers to the juristic cause.
3
Identify the opposing views
Ibn Abi Layla allows mudarabah with measurable/weighable goods (mithliyat) because they are also fungible. Malik allows it with commodities (urud) because they are valuable and commonly used in trade.
These are the main non-Hanafi positions.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response cites the hadith: 'The Messenger of Allah prohibited profit from what is not guaranteed.' Non-currency capital cannot be guaranteed by return because it is not specified (mu'ayyan) in the contract; the mudarib could return a different item, creating uncertainty (gharar).
This hadith is foundational in Hanafi muamalat.
5
Determine the practical consequence
In practice, a Hanafi judge would invalidate a mudarabah contract based on commodities, while a Maliki judge would uphold it. The thamarah al-khilaf is that the contract's validity depends on the capital type.

عروض (urud): Commodities, goods other than currency; in fiqh, often refers to non-fungible items.

Mudarabah capital must be currency (dirhams/dinars/fulus) because the hadith prohibits profit from unguaranteed items, and non-currency cannot be guaranteed by return.

Liability in Invalid Mudarabah and Condition of Capital
States two core rulings of mudarabah and expands on liability in fasid mudarabah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two core rulings of mudarabah regarding liability and capital, and explain the Hanafi position that the mudarib is not liable in fasid mudarabah, including the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, the mudarib is a trustee, not a guarantor — even if the contract is invalid, the capital remains a trust.

This passage introduces the foundational rules of mudarabah. It establishes that the mudarib is not liable for the capital, and that mudarabah requires capital valid for partnership. The commentary then delves into the critical question of liability in an invalid (fasid) mudarabah, revealing an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by the later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core rulings
Read the opening sentence: the capital is not guaranteed in mudarabah, and it is only valid with capital in which partnership is valid.
2
Analyze the first ruling: non-liability
The mudarib is a trustee (amin). Trustees do not guarantee loss unless they are negligent or commit transgression. This applies equally to valid and invalid mudarabah.
Key phrase: لأنه أمين
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Tahawi reports that Abu Hanifah's view is based on his position on the common hired worker (ajir mushtarak). The Sahibayn differ on the ajir mushtarak but later authorities agree on mudarib non-liability.
Analogy to ajir mushtarak
4
Resolve with the later tarjih
Al-Hindawani and al-Ispijabi state that there is consensus (at the level of the later school) that the mudarib does not guarantee because the property remains an amanah. This is the mufta bihi.
Al-Ispijabi declared it الأصح

Al-Ispijabi said: It is the most correct; because the property in the mudarib's hand is a trust, whether the mudarabah is valid or invalid; because the owner of capital intended that the property be with him as mudarabah, intended that he be a trustee, and he has authority for that. Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Mudarabah by stating two fundamental rulings. The first ruling: the capital is not guaranteed by the mudarib, meaning if the capital perishes without negligence, the mudarib is not liable. This applies equally to valid and invalid (fasid) mudarabah. The commentator explains that the mudarib is a trustee (amin), not a guarantor. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on this point. Al-Tahawi reports that Abu Hanifah holds this view, and it is based on the school's disagreement regarding the 'ajir mushtarak' (common hired worker). According to Abu Hanifah, a common hired worker does not guarantee property in his hand, and since the mudarib is analogous to a common hired worker (because he can accept mudarabah from multiple people), he likewise does not guarantee. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree on the common hired worker but here, Abu Hanifah's view is that there is no guarantee. Then Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani and al-Ispijabi assert that there is consensus that the mudarib does not guarantee in fasid mudarabah, because the property remains a trust (amanah) in his hand, as the owner's intention was for him to be a trustee. This is the relied-upon (asahh) position. The commentary also notes that the second condition for mudarabah is that it must be with capital in which partnership (shirkah) is valid. This will be elaborated in subsequent passages.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudarabah): A profit-sharing partnership where one party (rabb al-mal) provides capital and the other (mudarib) provides labor and expertise.
الأمين (al-amin): A trustee who is entrusted with property without guarantee for loss not due to his negligence.
الأجير المشترك (al-ajir al-mushtarak): A common hired worker who works for multiple people, like a tailor or porter; his liability for loss differs among Hanafi imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the mudarib's liability for the capital in a fasid (invalid) mudarabah?
A) The mudarib is liable for the capital because the contract is invalid.
B) The mudarib is not liable because the property is a trust (amanah) in his hand.
C) The mudarib is liable unless he obtains permission from the owner.
D) The mudarib is liable only if he commingles the funds.

Key Takeaway
In mudarabah, the mudarib is a trustee (amin) and does not guarantee the capital, whether the contract is valid or invalid; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Valid Capital in Mudarabah: Currency vs. Goods
Explains the condition that capital must be currency valid for partnership and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on valid mudarabah capital and evaluate the opposing arguments of Ibn Abi Layla and Malik using the hadith of 'profit from what is not guaranteed'.

Core Idea
Mudarabah capital must be currency; using goods leads to profit without liability, which is prohibited by hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya establishes a critical condition for a valid mudarabah contract: the capital must be currency (dirhams, dinars, or current coins), not goods or fungible items. We will explore the reasoning behind this condition and how the Hanafi school defends it against the views of Ibn Abi Layla and Imam Malik, relying on the prophetic prohibition of earning profit from what one does not guarantee.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact condition: mudarabah is not valid except with capital in which partnership is valid. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf), this means dirhams or dinars; according to Imam Muhammad, current coins (fulus ra'ijah) are also acceptable. Anything else is invalid by consensus.
The 'secret' (sirr) is that mudarabah is a partnership in profit, so it requires capital that is fungible and exactly determinable.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Ibn Abi Layla permits mudarabah with measurable/weighable goods (makil/mawzun) because they are of the same kind (dhawat al-amthal). Malik permits it with any goods (urud) because they are valuable and customarily used in trade.
Both opponents argue that the capital can be determined by estimation or market value.
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
For Ibn Abi Layla: makil/mawzun are fungible, so the capital can be specified by a similar quantity. For Malik: urud are valuable and commonly traded, so they serve the purpose of mudarabah like currency.
Malik also argues that if mudarabah is valid when already existing with goods, it should be valid from the start.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the hadith 'the Prophet forbade profit from what is not guaranteed'. When goods are used, the mudarib holds them as a trust (amanah). If he sells at a profit, the profit arises without the goods ever being in his liability (daman). In contrast, when purchasing with currency, the price becomes a debt in his dhimma. Goods become determined by specification (ta'ayyun), so the first transaction is a sale; profit from that sale without prior liability resembles hiring for sale with unknown wage, which is void.
The hadith is cited as a strong proof; it cannot be overridden by istihsan.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If mudarabah is done with goods, the contract is fasid (invalid) according to the Hanafis. Any profit belongs to the capital owner; the mudarib is entitled only to a fair wage (ujr al-mithl) for his work, not a share of profit.
The practical consequence: an investor cannot use goods as capital; doing so would require re-assessing the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا تصح إلا ... الخ ؛ يعني ولا تصح المضاربة إلا بمال تصح فيه الشركة، وهو أن يكون رأس المالِ دراهم أو دنانير عند الشيخين ، أو فلوساً رائجة عند محمد ه، حتى أنَّ المضاربة بما سوى هذه الأشياء لا تجوز إجماعاً.
والسر فيه : إنَّ المضاربة شركة في الربح ، فلا تصح إلا بما تصح به الشركة، وقال ابن أبي ليلى : تصح المضاربة في المكيل والموزون ؛ لأنها من ذواتِ الأمثال، فتقدير رأس المال بمثل المقبوض ممكن. وقال مالك : إن المضاربة تجوز بالعروض ؛ لكونها متقومة، يستريح عليها بالتجارة عادة، فصارت مثل النقدين فيما هو المقصود بالمضاربة، وتقدير رأس المال ممكن ؛ لكونها متقوّمة ؛ ولهذا تبقى المضاربة عليها، فجواز الابتداء بها أولى. والجواب : إنَّ رسول الله ﷺ نهى عن ربح ما لم يضمن»، والمضاربة بغيرِ النقودِ تؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن ؛ لكونها أمانة في يد المضارب، وقد تزداد قيمتها بعد الشراء، فإذا باعها شركة في الربح فحصل للمضارب ربح ما لم يضمن، فإنَّ المضارب يستحق نصيبه من غير دخولِ شيء في ضمانه، والنقود ليست كذلك، فإنّه عند شرائها يجب الثمنُ في ذمته ؛ لكونها غير متعينة بالتعيين والمكيل والموزون عروض. ألا ترى أنها تتعين بالتعيين، فأوّل تصرُّف يكون فيهما ليس إلا البيع، وقد حصل بهذا البيع ربح بأن باعه ثم رخص سعره بعد ذلك ، فظهر ربحه بدون الشراء، فيكون استئجاراً على البيع بأجرة مجهولة، فيكون باطلاً كما في العروض.
He said: 'And it is not valid except...' etc.; meaning mudarabah is not valid except with capital in which partnership is valid, and that is that the capital should be dirhams or dinars according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf), or current coins (fulus) according to Muhammad, such that mudarabah with anything other than these things is not permissible by consensus. The secret behind it is that mudarabah is a partnership in profit, so it is not valid except with that with which partnership is valid. Ibn Abi Layla said: Mudarabah is valid with measurable and weighable goods (makil and mawzun) because they are of the same kind (dhawat al-amthal), so determining the capital by a similar amount of the received goods is possible. Malik said: Mudarabah is permissible with goods (urud) because they are valuable (mutaqawwimah), one normally engages in trade with them, so they become like the two currencies in terms of the purpose of mudarabah, and determining the capital is possible because they are valuable; hence mudarabah continues upon them, so permitting it at the beginning is more appropriate. The answer: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ forbade profit from what is not guaranteed. Mudarabah with other than currency leads to profit from what is not guaranteed, because it is a trust in the hand of the mudarib, and its value may increase after purchase; if he sells it as a partnership in profit, then the mudarib gains profit from what he did not guarantee. The mudarib deserves his share without any portion entering his guarantee. Currency is not like that, because when it is purchased, the price becomes a debt in his liability (dhimma) since it is not determined by specification; whereas measurable and weighable goods are goods (urud); do you not see that they become determined by specification? So the first transaction in them is only sale, and by this sale profit is obtained if he sells it and then its price decreases afterwards, so his profit appears without purchase; thus it becomes a hire for sale with an unknown wage, so it is void (batil) as with goods.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage elaborates the second condition of mudarabah: the capital must be such that partnership (shirkah) is valid in it. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf), the capital must be dirhams or dinars (silver and gold coins). According to Imam Muhammad, it can also be current coins (fulus ra'ijah). There is consensus that mudarabah is not valid with other types of property. The reason (sirr) is that mudarabah is a partnership in profit, and partnership in profit is not valid except with fungible items that can be exactly measured. The text then presents two opposing views: Ibn Abi Layla permits mudarabah with measurable and weighable goods (makil and mawzun) because they are of the same kind, so the capital can be estimated. Imam Malik permits it with any goods (urud) because they are valuable and customarily used in trade. The Hanafi response is based on the hadith 'profit comes with liability' (al-kharaj bi al-daman) or the prohibition of profit from what is not guaranteed. When mudarabah is done with goods (not currency), the mudarib holds them as a trust. If he sells them at a higher price, he profits from the increase without the goods ever being in his liability (daman). In contrast, when buying with currency, the price becomes a debt in his dhimma; he is liable for it. Goods become determined by specification (ta'ayyun), so the first transaction is sale, and selling at a profit without prior purchase (where the price was in his liability) would be like hiring for sale with an unknown wage, which is void. Hence, mudarabah is only valid with currency.

Key Terms
فلوس رائجة (fulus ra'ijah): Current coins (copper or base metal) that are used as currency in the market.
عروض (urud): Goods (merchandise) other than currency and fungible items.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Items that can be replaced by similar items of the same kind, like wheat, barley, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof against using goods (urud) as capital for mudarabah?
A) Goods are not usable in trade.
B) The goods are not fungible (mithli).
C) The Prophet (ﷺ) forbade profit from what is not guaranteed (ربح ما لم يضمن).
D) Goods cannot be appraised.

Key Takeaway
Mudarabah capital must be currency because profit requires liability; goods do not satisfy this condition, rendering the contract fasid.

Mudarabah with Goods and Debt: Special Cases
Rulings on mudarabah capital when goods or debts are involved

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on three special cases of mudarabah capital involving goods and debt, and explain why the first two are valid while the third is invalid.

Core Idea
Mudarabah capital must be known and tangible; attaching the contract to a future receipt is valid in Hanafi law.

This passage clarifies three edge cases in mudarabah concerning the nature of capital. The first case involves goods given as capital, where the contract is tied to the future price. The second case involves a debt owed to the owner, collectible by the mudarib. The third case involves a debt already owed by the mudarib himself. The rulings hinge on whether capital is known and tangible at the time of the contract or at the time of operation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage presents three scenarios: (1) owner gives goods and says 'sell and do mudarabah with the price'; (2) owner says 'collect my debt from so-and-so and do mudarabah with it'; (3) owner says 'do mudarabah with the debt you owe me'.
Case 1 and 2 are valid; case 3 is invalid.
2
Analyze the first case: goods given as future capital
The mudarabah is not attached to the goods but to the price (thaman) after sale. Since the price will be currency, the capital is valid. The owner appoints the mudarib as agent to sell first, and the mudarabah contract is on the received price.
This contrasts with giving goods and saying 'value is X' – that is invalid because value is not fixed.
3
Analyze the second case: debt collected as capital
The owner says 'collect my debt and do mudarabah with it'. The contract is attached to the time of receipt; at that moment the debt becomes tangible ('ayn). Hanafi law allows attaching mudarabah to a future event, similar to wadi'ah, wakalah, and ijarah.
Thus the ruling: valid.
4
Analyze the third case: mudarib's own debt as capital
If the owner says 'do mudarabah with the debt you owe me', it is invalid. The mudarib cannot be a trustee over what he already owes; the debt is a guaranteed liability (mamnun) upon him, not a trust.
Key distinction: the mudarib is not a trustee over his own debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع رب المال إلى المضارب عرضاً ، وقال : بعه واعمل به مضاربة في ثمنه ، فباع بدراهم أو دنانير، وتصرف صح ؛ لأنه لم يضف المضاربة إلى العرض بل إلى ثمنه ، والثمن منهما يصح المضاربة به، وقال الشافعي له : بعدم جواز هذه المضاربة ؛ لإضافة عقد المضاربة إلى ما بعد البيع، وقبض الثمن.
والجواب : إنَّ رَبَّ المالِ وَكُلَ المضارب أولاً ببيع العروض، وهو كبيعه من نفسه، ثم عقد المضاربة على الثمن المقبوض ، وهو كالمقبوض في يده ، فوجب القولُ بجوازه ، نعم ؛ لو دفع إليه العروض على أنَّ قيمتها ألف درهم مثلاً ، ويكون ذلك رأس المال لا تجوزُ المضاربة حينئذ ؛ لأنَّ القيمةَ تختلف باختلافِ المقومين ، فلا يمكن ضبطها، فلا تصلح لرأس المال.
ولو قال رب المال للمضارب : اقبض مالي على فلان واعمل به مضاربة جاز ؛ لأنه أضاف المضاربة إلى زمان القبض، وفي ذلك الوقت يصير الدين عيناً، والمضاربة مما يقبلُ الإضافة إلى المستقبل ؛ لكونها وديعة أو وكالة أو إجارة، وشيء من ذلك لا يمنعُ فيه الإضافة إلى المستقبل.
ولو قال : اعمل بالدِّينِ الذي في ذمتك لا تصح المضاربة ؛ لأنَّ المضارب أمين ابتداء، ولا يتصور أن يكون الشخص أميناً فيما عليه من الدين؛ لأنَّ الدِّين مضمون على من هو عليه. كما في غير واحد من الكتب.
And if the owner of capital gave goods to the mudarib and said: 'Sell it and do mudarabah with its price,' and he sold it for dirhams or dinars and acted (in mudarabah), it is valid, because he did not attach the mudarabah to the goods but to its price, and the price from the two currencies makes mudarabah valid with it. Al-Shafi'i said in opposition: This mudarabah is not permissible, because the contract of mudarabah is attached to something after the sale and receipt of the price. The answer: The owner of capital first appointed the mudarib to sell the goods, which is like selling from himself, then his contract of mudarabah is on the received price, which is like what is received in his hand, so it is necessary to rule its permissibility. Yes, if he gave him the goods with the condition that their value is, for example, one thousand dirhams, and that is the capital, then mudarabah is not valid, because value differs by the difference of appraisers, so it cannot be fixed, so it is not suitable as capital. And if the owner of capital says to the mudarib: 'Accept my debt from so-and-so and do mudarabah with it,' it is valid, because he attached the mudarabah to the time of receipt, and at that time the debt becomes a tangible asset ('ayn), and mudarabah accepts attachment to the future, because it is like a deposit (wadi'ah), agency (wakalah), or hire (ijarah), and none of these prevent attachment to the future. And if he says: 'Do mudarabah with the debt that is in your liability (dhimma),' the mudarabah is not valid, because the mudarib is a trustee from the outset, and it is inconceivable that a person is a trustee over what is in his liability (debt), because the debt is guaranteed (mamnun) upon the one who owes it.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage addresses special cases of mudarabah involving goods and debts. First case: The owner gives the mudarib goods and says, 'Sell this and do mudarabah with its price.' This is valid according to Hanafis because the contract is attached to the price (thaman), which will be currency, not to the goods themselves. Al-Shafi'i objects, arguing that mudarabah is attached to a future event (the receipt of the price). The Hanafi response is that the owner first appoints the mudarib as an agent to sell the goods; this sale is like the owner selling it himself. After the sale, the price is in the mudarib's hand as if it were already received, so the mudarabah contract on that price is valid. However, if the owner simply gives goods and says their value is 1000 dirhams and that is the capital, it is invalid because the value of goods is not fixed and can vary among appraisers. Second case: The owner says to the mudarib, 'Collect my debt from so-and-so and do mudarabah with it.' This is valid because the mudarabah is attached to the time of receipt; the debt becomes a tangible asset ('ayn) when received, and mudarabah, like deposit, agency, or hire, can be attached to a future event. Third case: If the owner says to the mudarib, 'Do mudarabah with the debt that you owe me,' it is invalid because the mudarib cannot be a trustee over something he owes, as the debt is guaranteed (mamnun) upon him—he already owes it and it is in his liability, so it is not in his possession as a trust.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price; the currency received from sale, which becomes the capital of mudarabah.
دين (dayn): Debt; a liability owed by one person to another.
عين ('ayn): Tangible asset; property that is physically present (as opposed to a debt).
عرض ('ard): Goods; movable property other than currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the owner of capital says to the mudarib, 'Do mudarabah with the debt that you owe me,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Valid, because the debt becomes cash when collected.
B) Invalid, because the mudarib cannot be a trustee over what he owes.
C) Valid, because the mudarib is an agent for the owner.
D) Invalid, unless the debt is written down.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mudarabah capital can be the expected price from goods or a debt to be collected, but not a debt already owed by the mudarib himself.

Mudarabah with Currency vs Commodities: Profit without Guarantee
Explains the ruling with proof for mudarabah capital types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mudarabah with currency (naqd) is valid and with commodities (urud) is invalid according to the Hanafi school, and apply the ruling to determine whether a given capital is permissible.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, capital must be currency (naqd); commodities (urud) cannot be capital directly because they become specified, making profit without purchase akin to hiring with unknown wage.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between currency and commodities when forming a mudarabah contract. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the legal reasoning: currency, being fungible and not specified, allows the mudarib to earn profit without immediately guaranteeing the capital, while commodities become specific upon designation and thus require a sale first. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly structuring partnership contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage compares mudarabah with currency (naqd) and mudarabah with commodities (urud). Recognize that the ruling differs for each.
2
Understand the property of currency
Currency (dirhams and dinars) is fungible and not specified by designation. It remains a dayn (debt) until possessed, and the mudarib holds it as an amanah.
This means the mudarib does not owe the specific coins back, only the value.
3
Understand the property of commodities
Commodities like weighed or measured items become specified (muta'ayyina) upon designation. Once designated, they become a specific 'ayn in the contract.
This affects the nature of transactions: the first action with commodities must be sale.
4
Trace the legal consequence for commodities
If commodities are given as capital, the mudarib's first transaction is necessarily a sale. Profit from a price drop after sale (without prior purchase) is like hiring for sale with an unknown wage, which is invalid (fasid).
The profit does not come from the mudarib's labor in trading but from market fluctuation, which is uncertain.
5
Conclude the ruling
Mudarabah with currency is sahih (valid); with commodities directly it is fasid (invalid). To use commodities as capital, they must first be sold and the proceeds used as capital.
This is the established Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقودِ تؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن ؛ لكونها أمانة في يد المضارب، وقد تزداد قيمتها بعد الشراء، فإذا باعها شركة في الربح فحصل للمضارب ربح ما لم يضمن، فإنَّ المضارب يستحق نصيبه من غير دخولِ شيء في ضمانه، والنقود ليست كذلك، فإنّه عند شرائها يجب الثمنُ في ذمته ؛ لكونها غير متعينة بالتعيين والمكيل والموزون عروض. ألا ترى أنها تتعين بالتعيين، فأوّل تصرُّف يكون فيهما ليس إلا البيع، وقد حصل بهذا البيع ربح بأن باعه ثم رخص سعره بعد ذلك ، فظهر ربحه بدون الشراء، فيكون استئجاراً على البيع بأجرة مجهولة، فيكون باطلاً كما في العروض.
Currency leads to profit of what is not guaranteed; because it is an amanah in the hand of the mudarib, and its value may increase after purchase. If he sells it, there is partnership in profit, so the mudarib obtains profit of what he did not guarantee. Indeed, the mudarib deserves his share without anything entering his guarantee. Currency is not like that, because when it is purchased, the price becomes obligatory in his dhimma; because it is not specified by designation, and what is measured and weighed are commodities. Do you not see that they become specified by designation, so the first transaction in them is only sale. Profit has occurred by this sale when he sold it and its price later decreased, so his profit appears without purchase. Thus it becomes a hiring for sale with an unknown wage, so it is invalid as in the case of commodities.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage explains a key distinction in mudarabah between currency (naqd) and commodities (urud). The author states that currency, being fungible (non-specified), can generate profit for the mudarib without the mudarib becoming liable for the capital, because currency is an amanah in his hand. However, commodities like weighed or measured items become specific upon designation, so the first transaction is necessarily a sale. If profit occurs from a price drop after sale without an initial purchase, it is like hiring for sale with an unknown wage, which is invalid. The ruling is that mudarabah with commodities directly is invalid unless they are first sold and the proceeds used as capital.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profit shared as agreed.
النقود (al-nuqud): Currency (dirhams and dinars), which are fungible and not specified by designation in a contract.
العروض (al-urud): Commodities other than currency, such as goods measured or weighed, which become specific by designation.
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee/liability for the capital. In mudarabah, the mudarib does not guarantee the capital unless he transgresses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the mudarib cannot trade commodities
D) Because commodities are not permissible as capital in any contract

Key Takeaway
The validity of mudarabah capital depends on its fungibility: currency (naqd) is valid because it is not specified; commodities (urud) are invalid unless sold first.

Mudarabah on the Price of a Commodity: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on mudarabah via commodity sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on mudarabah when the owner gives a commodity to be sold first, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Mudarabah on the price after selling a commodity is valid because the sale is a separate agency and the price becomes suitable capital.

This passage presents a specific scenario: an owner gives a commodity to a mudarib, instructing him to sell it and then use the proceeds for mudarabah. The Hanafi school permits this, while al-Shafi'i objects on the grounds that the mudarabah contract is attached to a future event. We will examine the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences of the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the Hanafi ruling
Read the first part of the passage: the owner gives a commodity and says 'Sell it and do mudarabah with its price.' The Hanafi ruling is that this is valid.
Note that the mudarabah is attached to the price, not the commodity.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its basis
Al-Shafi'i argues that the mudarabah contract is attached to a future event (after sale and receipt), which is not permissible according to his school.
The objection is about the timing of the contract.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the owner first authorizes the sale as a separate agency (wakalah), then contracts mudarabah on the price which is now received and in the mudarib's hand.
The key distinction is that the authorization to sell is a separate, valid contract.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi school permits the transaction, Shafi'i does not. The Hanafi view is upheld as the relied-upon position.
This difference stems from different views on attaching contracts to future events.
5
Note the exceptional case and its reason
The passage ends with a case where the owner declares the commodity value as capital. This is invalid because value is uncertain and cannot be fixed as capital.
This reinforces that only the actual price received can serve as capital.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع رب المال إلى المضارب عرضاً ، وقال : بعه واعمل به مضاربة في ثمنه ، فباع بدراهم أو دنانير، وتصرف صح ؛ لأنه لم يضف المضاربة إلى العرض بل إلى ثمنه ، والثمن منهما يصح المضاربة به، وقال الشافعي له : بعدم جواز هذه المضاربة ؛ لإضافة عقد المضاربة إلى ما بعد البيع، وقبض الثمن. والجواب : إنَّ رَبَّ المالِ وَكُلَ المضارب أولاً ببيع العروض، وهو كبيعه من نفسه، ثم عقد المضاربة على الثمن المقبوض ، وهو كالمقبوض في يده ، فوجب القولُ بجوازه ، نعم ؛ لو دفع إليه العروض على أنَّ قيمتها ألف درهم مثلاً ، ويكون ذلك رأس المال لا تجوزُ المضاربة حينئذ ؛ لأنَّ القيمةَ تختلف باختلافِ المقومين ، فلا يمكن ضبطها، فلا تصلح لرأس المال.
If the owner of capital gives a commodity to the mudarib and says: 'Sell it and do mudarabah with its price,' and he sells it for dirhams or dinars and transacts, it is valid, because he did not attach the mudarabah to the commodity but to its price, and mudarabah is valid with the price. Al-Shafi'i said: This mudarabah is not permissible, because the mudarabah contract is attached to after the sale and receipt of the price. The answer: The owner first authorized the mudarib to sell the commodity, which is like selling it from himself, then he contracted mudarabah on the price received, which is like received in his hand, so it must be said to be permissible. Yes, if he gave him the commodity on the basis that its value is, for example, one thousand dirhams and that is the capital, then mudarabah is not permissible at that time, because value differs according to valuers, so it cannot be fixed, so it is not suitable as capital.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents a specific scenario: the owner gives a commodity to the mudarib, instructing him to sell it and then use the proceeds as capital for mudarabah. The Hanafi ruling is that this is valid because the mudarabah is attached to the price (thaman) after sale, not to the commodity itself. Imam al-Shafi'i objects on the grounds that the mudarabah contract is attached to a future event (after sale and receipt). The Hanafi response is that the owner first authorizes the sale (like a wakalah), which is separate, and then the mudarabah is on the already received price, which is in the mudarib's hand. The ruling distinguishes this from a case where the owner says the value of the commodity is X and that is the capital — that is invalid because value is uncertain.

Key Terms
العرض (al-ard): Commodity, good, as opposed to currency.
الثمن (al-thaman): Price, which in this context is the currency received from selling the commodity.
الوكالة (al-wakalah): Agency. Here, the owner appoints the mudarib to sell the commodity first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i in this passage, why is it valid to give a commodity to the mudarib to sell and then do mudarabah with the price?
A) Because the mudarabah contract can be attached to any future event
B) Because the authorization to sell is a separate agency, and the mudarabah is on the price after it is received in hand
C) Because the commodity is considered capital by itself
D) Because the Shafi'i school agrees with this ruling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits mudarabah on the price received from selling a commodity given by the owner, as long as the sale is a separate agency and the price is received in hand.

Mudarabah with a Debt to Be Collected
Ruling on mudarabah with outstanding debt as capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on mudarabah with a debt to be collected and apply the qiyas (analogy) to similar cases.

Core Idea
Mudarabah can be attached to a future event because it shares the illa with deposit, agency, and lease.

In this passage, we examine a specific scenario: the owner of capital has a debt owed by someone else, and instructs the mudarib to collect that debt and then use it as mudarabah capital. The Hanafi school rules that this is valid. The legal reasoning (ta'lil) is that mudarabah accepts attachment to the future, like other contracts that do not require immediate handover of the subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled on? What is the hukm (valid/invalid)? The act is mudarabah with capital that is a debt to be collected. The ruling is 'jaza' (permissible/valid).
2
Recognize the problem
Why is this case potentially problematic? Normally, mudarabah capital must be known and in hand. Here, the capital is a debt (dayn) which is not yet in hand.
The text says the debt becomes 'ayn upon collection.
3
Analyze the qiyas
The author analogizes mudarabah to wadi'ah (deposit), wakalah (agency), and ijara (lease). These contracts allow attachment to a future event. Identify the common element (illa): they all accept future attachment.
The illa is not explicitly named but is derived: acceptance of future time.
4
Apply the analogy
Since mudarabah shares this illa, its ruling is the same as those contracts: valid to attach to a future event.
5
Note the epistemological weight
This is the established Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah). Other schools may differ (require capital in hand at contract). But within the Hanafi school, it is mufta bihi.
Refer to the hanafi_position_box for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال رب المال للمضارب : اقبض مالي على فلان واعمل به مضاربة جاز ؛ لأنه أضاف المضاربة إلى زمان القبض، وفي ذلك الوقت يصير الدين عيناً، والمضاربة مما يقبلُ الإضافة إلى المستقبل ؛ لكونها وديعة أو وكالة أو إجارة، وشيء من ذلك لا يمنعُ فيه الإضافة إلى المستقبل.
If the owner of capital says to the mudarib: 'Collect my debt from so-and-so and do mudarabah with it,' it is permissible, because he attached the mudarabah to the time of collection, and at that time the debt becomes an 'ayn, and mudarabah accepts attachment to the future because it is like a deposit, agency, or lease, and none of these prevent attachment to the future.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on a scenario where the owner has a debt owed by a third party and instructs the mudarib to collect that debt and then use it as capital for mudarabah. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible. The reason: the mudarabah contract is attached to the future time of collection, and at that moment the debt becomes a physical asset (ayn) in the mudarib's hand. Mudarabah, like wadi'ah (deposit), wakalah (agency), and ijara (lease), can be attached to a future event without prohibition. Therefore, the contract is valid.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): Debt, which is a liability in the dhimma. Upon collection, it becomes an 'ayn.
العين (al-'ayn): Physical asset, property that can be possessed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the debt is already in hand
B) Because mudarabah can be attached to the future time of collection, and the debt becomes an 'ayn at that time
C) Because the mudarib is liable for the debt
D) Because the owner has already received the debt

Key Takeaway
Mudarabah can be validly contracted with a debt to be collected because it shares the legal effective cause with contracts that permit future attachment.

Mudarabah with the Mudarib's Own Debt: Invalid Due to Trusteeship Conflict
Ruling that mudarabah with the mudarib's own debt is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mudarabah with the mudarib's own debt and explain the underlying principle that a person cannot be a trustee (amin) over a liability he owes.

Core Idea
A person cannot be a trustee over a debt he owes; mudarabah capital must be an actual trust asset.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: an investor tells the mudarib to use a debt that the mudarib himself owes to the investor as capital for a mudarabah. The Hanafi school rules this contract invalid. The reasoning is grounded in the nature of trusteeship (amanah) and liability (daman). Understanding this ruling clarifies a fundamental boundary in Islamic partnership contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Clearly describe the hypothetical contract: the rabb al-mal (investor) says to the mudarib: 'Do mudarabah with the debt that you owe me.' The mudarib is both debtor and potential trustee.
The debt is already in the mudarib's dhimma; no asset is physically transferred.
2
State the Ruling
State that the mudarabah is invalid (batil) according to the Hanafi school. This is a clear, unanimous position within the madhhab.
Hukm level: batil (not merely fasid).
3
Analyze the Legal Reason (Illah)
The text gives the reason: the mudarib is an amin (trustee) from the inception of the contract, and it is legally impossible for someone to be a trustee over his own debt because a debt is a liability (daman) upon the debtor, not a trust asset in his possession.
The mudarib cannot simultaneously be the debtor (liable) and the trustee (amin) over the same amount.
4
Confirm the Principle
Recognize the general Hanafi principle: in mudarabah, the mudarib must be an amin over the capital. A debt owed by the mudarib himself cannot serve as capital because it is not a tangible asset held in trust.
This principle is affirmed across multiple Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال : اعمل بالدينِ الذي في ذمتك لا تصح المضاربة ؛ لأنَّ المضارب أمين ابتداء، ولا يتصور أن يكون الشخص أميناً فيما عليه من الدين؛ لأنَّ الدِّين مضمون على من هو عليه. كما في غير واحد من الكتب.
If he says: 'Do mudarabah with the debt that is in your dhimma,' the mudarabah is not valid, because the mudarib is a trustee (amin) from the beginning, and it is inconceivable that a person be a trustee over what is owed by him, because the debt is a liability on the one who owes it. As is stated in many books.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage gives a clear ruling: if the owner instructs the mudarib to use a debt that the mudarib himself owes to the owner as capital for mudarabah, it is invalid. The reason is that the mudarib is an amin (trustee) for the capital in mudarabah, but he cannot be a trustee over something that is a liability (daman) on him. A debt owed by the mudarib is a responsibility on him, not an asset in his hands that he can manage as a trust. Therefore, such a mudarabah is not valid.

Key Terms
الأمين (al-amin): Trustee; one who holds property without liability for loss unless negligent.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal personality or liability. A debt is in the dhimma of the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is mudarabah with the mudarib's own debt invalid?
A) Because the debt is not a valid asset
B) Because the mudarib cannot be a trustee over a liability that he owes
C) Because the mudarabah contract is not valid with any debt
D) Because the owner must be the debtor

Key Takeaway
A mudarabah contract is invalid when the capital is a debt owed by the mudarib himself, because he cannot serve as trustee over his own liability.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate

Conditions for Valid Mudarabah: Delivery, Profit Sharing, and Prohibition of Fixed Profit
Matn and sharh on three essential conditions for mudarabah validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three essential conditions for a valid mudarabah contract and explain why stipulating a fixed profit invalidates it, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mudarabah is valid only when capital is delivered, profit is shared proportionally, and no fixed sum is guaranteed.

This passage from the matn and its sharh lays out the core conditions that make a mudarabah contract valid in the Hanafi school. Understanding these conditions is crucial because any violation leads to a fasid (invalid) contract, but the mudarib still receives compensation (ujr al-mithl) for his work. The commentary clarifies that not every invalid condition destroys the contract; only those that cut off partnership or cause ignorance of profit do so.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions from the matn
The matn directly states: delivery to the mudarib, sharing of profit, and the prohibition of a fixed increase for either party.
The sharh expands each condition with examples.
2
Understand the rationale behind each condition
Delivery ensures exclusive control (yad amanah). Profit sharing (shuyu' al-ribh) is necessary for partnership. A fixed sum cuts off partnership if the profit equals that sum.
The rationale is based on the nature of mudarabah as a partnership in profit.
3
Distinguish between invalidating conditions and void conditions
Conditions that cut off partnership or cause ignorance of profit invalidate the entire contract. Other invalid conditions (e.g., loss on mudarib) are void but the contract remains valid.
This distinction is important for determining the consequences.
4
Determine the consequence when the mudarabah is invalid
If the contract is invalid due to a condition that cuts off partnership, the mudarib still gets ujr al-mithl (equivalent wage) for his work, as his benefits are enjoyed.
The contract is fasid, not batil, so some compensation is due.

Source Text

Book of Mudarabah: Its definition and rulings. And with its delivery to the mudarib. And sharing of profit between them. So it is invalid if a fixed increase is stipulated for one of them. [1] 2. And with its delivery to the mudarib. And sharing of profit between them, so it is invalid if a fixed increase is stipulated for one of them. Know that every condition that cuts off partnership in profit or causes ignorance of profit invalidates it. Other invalid conditions that invalidate a sale do not invalidate mudarabah, but that condition is void, and likewise the condition that loss is on the mudarib. [1] His statement: 'And with its delivery...' means mudarabah is not valid except with delivery of capital to the mudarib, because his hand over the capital is a hand of trust, so mudarabah is not completed except by delivery to him like a deposit. So it is necessary that the capital be exclusively in his hand to enable him to transact. Therefore, if the owner stipulates that the owner work with the mudarib, the mudarabah is invalid. For stipulating the owner's work prevents exclusive control of the mudarib, so the mudarib cannot transact and the purpose is not achieved, whether the owner is a party or not. Like when the father or guardian gives the child's property as mudarabah and stipulates the child's work, the child's hand over the property is by ownership like an adult, and the retention of his hand prevents the property from being delivered to the mudarib. [2] His statement: 'And sharing of profit between them' in the genitive, coordinated with 'delivery'; mudarabah is not valid except with sharing of profit between owner and mudarib, e.g., one-third or half. For partnership is only achieved by that. If he stipulates a named sum for one of them, it invalidates the mudarabah because it leads to cutting off the partnership if the profit does not exceed that sum. [3] His statement: 'So it is invalid...' because stipulating that cuts off the partnership between them; perhaps he will not profit except that amount, so it cuts off partnership in profit. When it becomes invalid, its benefits remain enjoyed by virtue of the contract, so the equivalent wage is due. [4] His statement: 'Know...' summary: Every condition that implies cutting off partnership or causes ignorance of profit necessitates the invalidity of mudarabah. Other conditions do not invalidate it, but the condition is void, like stipulating loss on the mudarib.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from the matn and its sharh outlines three essential conditions for a valid mudarabah contract. First, the capital must be delivered to the mudarib (taslim). The mudarib's hand is a trust hand, so full delivery is necessary for him to have exclusive control; if the owner stipulates that he himself will also work, the mudarib's control is compromised and the contract is invalid. Second, profit must be shared between the owner and mudarib in a known proportion, like one-third or half. If a fixed sum is stipulated for either party, it invalidates the contract because it may cut off partnership in profit. Third, if a fixed increase is stipulated for one party (e.g., ten dirhams), the mudarabah is invalid because it also cuts off partnership. The commentary adds that any condition that severs partnership or creates ignorance of profit invalidates the contract, but other invalid conditions (like those that would invalidate a sale) do not invalidate mudarabah; only the condition is void. The condition that loss is on the mudarib is also void. If the mudarabah is invalid, the benefits are compensated by paying the mudarib an equivalent wage (ujr al-mithl).

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery of the capital from the owner to the mudarib, giving him exclusive control.
شيوع الربح (shuyu' al-ribh): The profit must be a common share (e.g., half, third) between the partners, not a fixed sum.
الوضيعة (al-wadi'ah): Loss. The condition that loss is on the mudarib is void because loss by nature is on the capital owner.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): The equivalent wage for the mudarib's work when the mudarabah is fasid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence if the owner stipulates a fixed profit of ten dirhams for himself in a mudarabah contract?
A) The mudarabah is invalid, and the mudarib gets nothing
B) The mudarabah is invalid, and the mudarib gets ujr al-mithl
C) The condition is void but the mudarabah remains valid
D) The mudarabah is valid but the profit is shared proportionally

Key Takeaway
For a mudarabah to be valid, capital must be delivered, profit must be shared in a known proportion, and no fixed sum can be guaranteed; violation makes the contract fasid with ujr al-mithl owed to the mudarib.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Conditions for Valid Mudarabah: Delivery, Profit Sharing, Invalid Conditions
Explaining the three conditions for a valid mudarabah contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid mudarabah and identify which conditions invalidate the contract versus those that are merely void.

Core Idea
Mudarabah requires delivery of capital and proportional profit-sharing; any condition breaking partnership corrupts the contract.

This lesson unpacks the three pillars of a valid mudarabah from a classical Hanafi text. You will learn why delivery of capital is essential, why profit must be shared proportionally, and how certain conditions can either corrupt the contract or be nullified while the contract remains valid. These rules ensure the partnership nature of mudarabah is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the passage and list the three conditions explicitly mentioned: delivery of capital, profit-sharing proportionally, and absence of conditions that break partnership or cause ignorance of profit.
The text states 'And with its delivery to the mudarib. And the sharing of profit between them, so it is corrupted if a fixed extra amount is stipulated for one of them.'
2
Understand delivery requirement
Analyze why delivery is necessary: the mudarib holds capital as a trust (amanah) and needs exclusive control to transact. If the owner also works, the mudarib's hand is not exclusive, corrupting the contract.
The commentary gives the example of a guardian stipulating the minor's work.
3
Understand profit-sharing requirement
Profit must be a common share (e.g., halves, thirds). A fixed sum invalidates because it may cut off partnership if profit equals that sum.
The text says 'if a fixed amount of dirhams is stipulated... the mudarabah is invalidated'.
4
Recognize invalid conditions
Any condition that suggests cutting off partnership or causes ignorance of profit corrupts the mudarabah. Other invalid conditions (like those in sale) do not corrupt but are simply void.
Example: stipulating loss on the mudarib is void, but the contract remains valid.
5
Apply the distinction
Practice by analyzing a hypothetical condition: if the owner stipulates that the mudarib must return the capital plus a fixed 10% profits, this corrupts the contract because it fixes profit instead of sharing proportionally.
Contrast with a condition like 'the mudarib must trade only in textiles' – that is not profit-related, so it is void but does not invalidate.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement outlining the conditions for a valid mudarabah contract. The author first states the three key conditions: (1) delivery of capital to the mudarib, (2) sharing of profit between owner and mudarib, and (3) the invalidity of stipulating a fixed extra amount for one party. The commentary expands on each: Delivery is necessary because the mudarib holds the capital as a trust (amanah), and without delivery, the contract is incomplete like a deposit (wadi'ah). If the owner stipulates that he himself works with the mudarib, it corrupts the contract because it prevents the mudarib from having exclusive control over the capital. Similarly, if a guardian gives a minor's property as mudarabah and stipulates the minor's work, the minor's ownership hand prevents delivery. For profit-sharing, it must be proportional (e.g., 1/3, 1/2); a fixed sum invalidates the contract because it severs partnership if profit does not exceed that sum. The third point generalizes: any condition that suggests cutting off partnership or creates ignorance about profit corrupts the mudarabah. Other invalid conditions (like those that corrupt a sale) do not corrupt the mudarabah but are nullified; for example, stipulating that the mudarib bears losses is an invalid condition but does not invalidate the contract itself.

Key Terms
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A partnership contract where one party (rabb al-mal) provides capital and the other (muḍārib) provides labor, with profit shared according to agreement.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
شيوع الربح (shuyūʿ al-ribḥ): Profit-sharing in proportion (e.g., fractions) rather than a fixed sum, ensuring partnership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the owner stipulates that the mudarib must pay a fixed amount of profit regardless of actual profit?
A) The mudarabah is valid but the condition is void.
B) The mudarabah is corrupted (fasid).
C) The condition is valid and binding.
D) The mudarabah becomes a loan.

Key Takeaway
A valid mudarabah requires delivery of capital, proportional profit-sharing, and no conditions that break partnership or cause ignorance of profit.

Mudarib's Authority to Sell on Credit in Absolute Mudarabah
Inter-madhhab dispute on credit sales in absolute mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the mudarib's authority to sell on credit in an absolute mudarabah and evaluate the opposing madhhab's argument.

Core Idea
In absolute mudarabah, the mudarib may sell on credit because it is the merchants' custom and maximizes profit.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah examines a key dispute: may a mudarib (agent) in an unrestricted mudarabah sell goods on credit without explicit permission from the capital owner? The Hanafi school says yes, citing trade custom and profit maximization. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools require permission. Understanding this dispute clarifies how custom ('urf) and contractual objectives shape Hanafi rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the ruling from the matn: the mudarib may sell for cash or credit, except for a term not customary. Understand that this is the default in an absolute mudarabah.
The matn's generality is key.
2
Locate the opposing position
Identify the view of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration): selling on credit requires the owner's permission.
Opposing dalil: credit sale restricts the mudarib's hand, akin to a new mudarabah.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (wa lana)
The Hanafi response: selling on credit is the practice of merchants and yields higher profit, fulfilling the owner's goal. This is a dalil aqli based on custom and objective.
The commentary cites al-Shumnunni that credit sales are more profitable.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa allows selling at an unconscionable price or for an unusual term; his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disallow it. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
Qadi Khan's Fatawa is cited as authority.
5
Apply the ruling to the scope of absolute mudarabah
Understand that 'absolute' means unrestricted by place, time, commodity, or person. If restrictions are stipulated (e.g., cash only), the mudarib must comply by agreement.
The text distinguishes stipulations on cash vs credit.

The cream of the finale for 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 354 And for the mudarib in an absolute mudarabah to sell for cash and credit except for a term not customary, and to buy. [1] His statement: 'And for the mudarib...' etc.; meaning: it is permissible for the mudarib in an absolute mudarabah to sell for cash and credit; because the contract is absolute and the purpose is profit, and that is not achieved except through trade, so the contract encompasses all types of trade. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the mudarib's authority to sell on credit in an absolute (unrestricted) mudarabah. The matn states that the mudarib may sell for cash or credit, except for a term not customary, and may buy. The commentary explains that this is because the contract is absolute and aims for profit through trade. The opposing view from Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration) is that selling on credit requires explicit permission from the capital owner, since it constrains the mudarib's ability to transact. The Hanafi response (wa lana) is that selling on credit is the practice of merchants and yields greater profit, which is the owner's objective. The commentary then clarifies that 'absolute mudarabah' means unrestricted by place, time, commodity, or person. It also notes an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa permits selling at an unconscionable price or for an unusual term, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not. The passage also discusses buying: the mudarib may buy customary goods, but if he buys at a price beyond normal haggling, he is considered a violator. The text ends abruptly but the rule is clear.

Key Terms
مطلق المضاربة (muṭlaq al-muḍārabah): An absolute mudarabah with no restrictions on time, place, commodity, or person; the mudarib has broad authority.
نسيئة (nasīʾah): Selling on credit/deferred payment.
ما لا يتغابن الناس فيه (mā lā yataghāban al-nās fīh): A price so off the market that people would not normally accept it; an unconscionable price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In an absolute mudarabah, the mudarib may sell on credit without the owner's permission because it is customary and profit-maximizing, but must avoid unconscionable prices or non-customary terms.

Mudarib's Right to Sell on Credit in Absolute Mudarabah
Explains mudarib's authority to sell on credit and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a mudarib's authority to sell on credit in an absolute mudarabah and contrast it with the opposing school's proof and practical consequence.

Core Idea
In absolute mudarabah, custom and profit-maximization empower the mudarib to sell on credit without permission.

This lesson unpacks a key dispute: does a mudarib (agent in a profit-sharing venture) need explicit permission to sell on credit? The Hanafi school says no, citing trade custom and profit goals. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools require permission. We will trace the reasoning on both sides and understand the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the commentary: the mudarib in an absolute mudarabah may sell on credit without explicit permission.
The source text states: 'وللمضارب في مطلقها أن يبيع بنقد ونسيئة'.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
The opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) argue that selling on credit restricts the mudarib's ability to transact further and resembles a new mudarabah contract, requiring the owner's consent.
Their dalil: 'البيع بالنسيئة يوجب قصر يد المضارب عن التصرف، فيصير بمنزلة دفع المال مضاربة'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (لنا) is that selling on credit is customary among merchants and more profitable, which is the very objective of the capital owner.
Customary practice and profit maximization are sufficient to include credit sales under the absolute contract.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the Hanafi view, the mudarib has more flexibility; under the opposing view, he must seek permission, limiting his autonomy.
This difference affects day-to-day business decisions and the speed of transactions.
5
Note qualifications
If the owner stipulates cash-only, the mudarib cannot sell on credit by agreement. If the owner stipulates credit-only, the mudarib may still sell for cash if the price is fair.
The absolute nature is key: restrictions by the owner override the default.

And for the mudarib in an absolute [mudarabah], he may sell for cash and on credit, except for a term that is not customary. And he may buy... [Commentary] He said: 'For the mudarib... etc.' meaning it is permissible for the mudarib in the absolute mudarabah to sell for cash and on credit, because the contract is absolute and its objective is profit, which is only achieved through trade, so the contract encompasses types of trade. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad [said] in one narration: He may not sell on credit except with the permission of the capital owner, because selling on credit causes the mudarib's hand to be restricted from acting, making it akin to handing over the money as a mudarabah, so it is not permissible without the owner's permission. Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, explaining the mudarib's authority in an absolute mudarabah contract. The author first states the ruling from the matn: the mudarib in an absolute mudarabah may sell for cash or on credit, except for a term that is not customary, and he may buy and appoint agents and travel. The commentary then expands on selling. The ruling is that in an absolute mudarabah (where no restrictions are placed on the mudarib), the mudarib has the right to sell both for cash and on credit because the contract is unrestricted and the purpose is profit, which requires trade in all its forms. The commentator then presents the position of Imams al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one narration): they hold that selling on credit is not allowed without explicit permission from the capital owner. Their reasoning is that selling on credit restricts the mudarib from further transactions (since payment is deferred and the mudarib may not have funds to continue trading), so it resembles a new mudarabah contract on the debt, which requires the owner's consent. The Hanafi response (indicated by 'لنا' - our proof) is that selling on credit is the established practice of merchants and is more profitable, which is the very objective of the capital owner. The commentator adds that the mudarib's authority is qualified: if the owner stipulated cash sales only, the mudarib must comply; but if he stipulated credit sales, the mudarib may still sell for cash as long as the price is not significantly less than market value (ghabn fahish). If a dispute arises about whether the sale was cash or credit, the mudarib's word is accepted in mudarabah (since he is the trustee) and similarly the agent's word in agency.

Key Terms
مطلق المضاربة (mutlaq al-mudarabah): An absolute mudarabah contract where the capital owner imposes no restrictions on the mudarib regarding time, place, type of trade, or specific parties.
بيع نسيئة (bay' al-nasi'ah): A credit sale where payment is deferred to a future date.
قصر يد (qasr al-yad): Restriction of the mudarib's ability to transact further.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the mudarib is the owner of the capital.
B) Because selling on credit is customary and more profitable, fulfilling the contract's purpose.
C) Because the capital owner explicitly allows it in all cases.
D) Because the mudarib is not liable for losses.

Key Takeaway
In an absolute mudarabah, the mudarib's authority is broad and includes selling on credit without permission, as it aligns with trade custom and profit maximization.

Mudarib's Rights to Buy, Appoint Agents, and Travel (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Explains mudarib's authority with conditions and internal debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the mudarib's buying, agency, and travel, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on travel with reference to the relevant proofs.

Core Idea
The mudarib's authority is not absolute; buying must be customary, travel is conditional, and intra-Hanafi disagreement refines the ruling.

The mudarib in an absolute mudarabah contract possesses significant authority to conduct trade, but this authority is bounded by customary practice and the inherent risks of capital deployment. This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah clarifies three key rights – buying, appointing agents, and traveling – and then dives into a nuanced internal Hanafi debate regarding the mudarib's right to travel with the capital, offering you a window into how classical scholars balanced contractual intent with practical exigency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Rights
Locate the three activities the passage permits the mudarib to do: buying, appointing agents (for cash and credit), and traveling with the capital. Note that these are defaults in an absolute mudarabah.
2
Analyze the Condition for Buying
Examine the restriction: the mudarib must buy what is customary among traders. If he buys at a grossly inflated price (ghabn fahish), he is in violation even if given unrestricted permission. Also note exclusion of buying from his own authorized slave or mukatab.
This condition protects against misuse even in an unrestricted contract.
3
Trace the Travel Dispute Structure
The passage presents three views: (1) Zahir al-riwayah: travel is permissible unconditionally; (2) Abu Yusuf: not allowed; (3) Abu Hanifa: conditional – allowed only if capital was given outside the mudarib's hometown, so he can return. Identify the reasoning behind each.
Abu Yusuf analogizes to wadi'ah; Abu Hanifa implies owner's consent from context.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Based on the text's presentation and later Hanafi preference, the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's conditional view, which balances the mudarib's need to travel with the protection of the capital.
The text presents zahir al-riwayah as the default but then provides the more detailed view as a refinement.

[3] He said: 'And with Abu Yusuf... etc.' He said in Al-Hidayah: 'From Abu Yusuf: he may not travel.' End. He said in Al-Kifayah: 'This is for what has carriage and expense, based on his statement in wadi'ah.' Likewise in Al-Mabsut. End. And he said in Al-Hidayah: 'From Abu Hanifa: if [the capital] was handed over in his own town, he may not travel because it is exposure to destruction without necessity; if handed over outside his town, he may travel to his town because that is the intended purpose in most cases.' End. And the justification for traveling when given outside his town is that the apparent consent of the capital owner is present, because a person generally cannot stay in a foreign land permanently, so giving the capital as a mudarabah in this situation indicates consent to travel to his town.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the absolute mudarabah, covering three additional rights: buying, appointing agents, and traveling. First, the mudarib may buy what is customary among traders. However, if he buys at a price significantly above market value (ghabn fahish), he is in violation, even if the capital owner gave him unrestricted authority. Also, he cannot buy from his own authorized slave (ma'dhun) or from his mukatab (slave contracted for freedom) by agreement. Second, the mudarib may appoint agents to carry out buying and selling, both for cash and credit, since appointing agents is also a common commercial practice. Third, the mudarib may travel with the capital; this is the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah). The reason is that the word 'mudarabah' derives from 'striking the earth' (to travel for trade), so the absolute contract inherently grants travel authority. The text then presents intra-Hanafi differences. According to Abu Yusuf, the mudarib may not travel at all (because of risks and expenses). According to Abu Hanifa, there is a distinction: if the capital was given in the mudarib's own town, he may not travel (as it is needlessly exposing the capital to risk); but if it was given in a foreign town where the mudarib is staying, he may travel back to his own town, because the owner likely intended that. The justification is that the owner would not expect the mudarib to remain forever in a foreign place, so giving capital there implies consent to travel back.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparently transmitted ruling from Imam Muhammad's six books, considered the most authoritative within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Abu Hanifa's justification for allowing the mudarib to travel when the capital was given outside his hometown?
A) The mudarib needs to diversify investment locations.
B) The apparent consent of the capital owner is present because no one stays in a foreign land permanently.
C) Travel is always safer than staying in one place.
D) The mudarib has absolute authority under the contract.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's authority in absolute mudarabah includes buying customary goods, appointing agents, and travel, but travel is subject to intra-Hanafi conditions; the relied-upon view allows travel only when the capital was given outside the mudarib's hometown.

Mudarib's Right to Appoint Agents and Travel in Absolute Mudarabah
Ruling with proof on mudarib's travel and agency appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on mudarib appointing agents and traveling in absolute mudarabah and distinguish between the different Hanafi positions.

Core Idea
In absolute mudarabah, the mudarib may appoint agents and travel; other views exist but permissibility is the relied position.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the mudarib's authority in an absolute mudarabah. While the default is permissibility to appoint agents and travel, there are nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreements, particularly on travel. Let's examine the arguments and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling for appointing agents
The text states that the mudarib may appoint agents for buying and selling on cash and credit because this is standard trade practice ('urf al-tujjar). This is uncontested within the Hanafi school.
The permissibility extends to both cash and credit transactions.
2
Identify the default ruling for traveling
The text states that traveling with the capital is permissible (zahir al-riwayah). The reasoning is linguistic: the term mudarabah is derived from 'darb fi al-ard' (traveling in the land).
This is the apparent ruling from the core Hanafi texts.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the mudarib may not travel because it exposes the capital to destruction without necessity, analogous to his view on deposits (wadi'ah).
He analogizes to the depositary who also cannot travel with the deposit if it requires carrying and provision.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's differentiated view
According to a narration from Abu Hanifa, if the capital was given in the mudarib's own city, travel is not allowed; if given in another city, travel to his city is allowed because of implied consent.
The reasoning: a person does not usually stay in a foreign land permanently, so giving capital there implies consent to travel home.
5
Analyze the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentator defends the permissibility by rejecting the fear of destruction as unfounded (safety is the norm) and by drawing a stronger analogy: a depositary may travel with a deposit, so a mudarib is even more entitled. The permissibility is the mufta bihi position.
The commentator also notes a linguistic nuance: the word 'inda (according to) in the text is criticized because it suggests a madhhab position, whereas these are individual narrations.

And he appoints agents for both (buying and selling) and travels. He appoints agents: i.e., for buying and selling, and travels. According to Abu Yusuf: he may not travel. According to Abu Hanifa: if the capital was handed over in his own city, he may not travel; if in another city, he may travel to his city. Act on your opinion, or he did not say. End. And he does not buy from his authorized slave, and it is said: from his mukatab by agreement. Al-Qahustani mentioned it in Sharh al-Niqayah. [1] His saying: 'And he appoints agents' means it is permissible for the mudarib in absolute mudarabah to appoint agents for buying and selling on cash and credit, because appointment of agents is from the practices of merchants. [2] His saying: 'And he travels' means it is permissible for the mudarib in absolute mudarabah to travel with the capital of the mudarabah, and this is the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwayah), as stated in al-Hidayah and others. Its reasoning is that mudarabah is derived from traveling in the land, so he owns it by the absolute contract since the word indicates it. (3) His saying: 'According to Abu Yusuf...' etc. He said in al-Hidayah: From Abu Yusuf: he may not travel. End. He said in al-Kifayah: This is regarding that which has carrying and provision, based on his statement regarding the deposit. So in al-Mabsut. End. (0) And he said in al-Hidayah: From Abu Hanifa: if he handed over in his city, he may not travel because it is exposure to destruction without necessity; if he handed over in another city, he may travel to his city because that is generally intended. End. The reasoning for travel: if handed over in another city, the apparent consent of the capital owner, for a person does not usually stay in a foreign land permanently, so giving the capital as mudarabah in this situation indicates his consent to travel to his city. The answer to this narration: It is an exposure to destruction which is not conceded, for the apparent is safety, and what is imagined is not considered. Do you not see that the depositor may travel with the deposit while he may not deposit it, and the mudarib has that, so it is more appropriate and more deserving of permissibility. In Fatawa Qadi Khan: Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that he does not travel with it at all. End. So it becomes clear that what the commentator mentioned by his saying 'he may not travel' and by his saying 'according to Abu Hanifa: if he handed over...' are narrations from them, not their madhhab, as the word 'inda (according to) indicates, for it is only mentioned for the madhhab, so the appropriate word would be 'an (from) not 'inda; so reflect.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage covers the mudarib's rights in an absolute mudarabah. First, the mudarib may appoint agents for buying and selling on credit and cash, as this is standard trade practice. Second, he may travel with the capital. This is the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah). Abu Yusuf held he may not travel, akin to his view on deposits. Abu Hanifa differentiated: if the capital was given in his own city, he may not travel (unnecessary risk), but if given in another city, he may travel to his own city (implied consent). The commentator defends the permissibility, noting that safety is assumed and unreasonable fear is disregarded. He analogizes to the depositary who may travel with the deposit whereas he may not re-deposit. A report from Abu Hanifa via Abu Yusuf says he may not travel at all. The commentator notes that using 'inda (according to) was inappropriate because it implies a madhhab position, while these are individual narrations.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudārabah): A sleeping partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor for profit-sharing.
مطلقة (muṭlaqah): Absolute, unrestricted mudarabah where no specific conditions are placed on the mudarib's actions.
التوكيل (al-tawkīl): Appointment of an agent to act on one's behalf

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if the capital was given in a city other than his own.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib in an absolute mudarabah may appoint agents and travel, as these are integral to trade practice, despite differing narrations.
