Permissibility of Mudarib Giving Capital as Bida'ah to the Capital Owner
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on mudarib giving capital as bida'ah to the capital owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the mudarib giving capital as bida'ah to the capital owner and distinguish Zufar's opposing view, including the reasoning (dalil) for each side.

Core Idea
The mudarib's right of disposal allows him to delegate authority, even to the capital owner.

In a mudarabah contract, the mudarib is entrusted with the capital to trade. However, can he give that same capital to the capital owner himself to trade on his behalf? This is a practical scenario that raises questions about agency and revocation. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that such an act is permissible and does not invalidate the mudarabah, addressing a contrary view from the Hanafi scholar Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Define 'ibḍā‘' in this context
Note that the author clarifies 'ibḍā‘' here means merely seeking assistance, not the full bida'ah contract where the capital owner retains all profit. The mudarib is simply using the capital owner as a helper in trade.
This narrow definition is crucial to understanding why the mudarabah remains valid.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The mudarib may give the capital as bida'ah to anyone, including the capital owner himself, and the mudarabah is not invalidated by this act.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and the majority of Hanafi scholars.
3
Identify Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that the mudarabah is invalidated because the capital owner, when receiving the capital as bida'ah, is disposing for himself and cannot be the mudarib's agent in that disposal, making it a revocation of the mudarabah.
Zufar's view is recorded as an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi response is that the right of disposal in the mudarabah belongs to the mudarib. Therefore, the capital owner can act as the mudarib's agent in that disposal, just as the mudarib can order the owner to buy or sell. Hence, no revocation occurs.
This qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) upholds the mudarib's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱] قوله: ويبضع.... الخ; يعني وجاز للمضارب في المضاربة المطلقة أن يعطي المال بضاعة للتجارة، ولو أعطاه المضارب ربَّ المال، والإبضاعُ: هو أن يكون المال للمبضع، والعمل من آخر، والربح كله لرب المال. وقال البرْجُنْدِي: والمراد بالإيضاع هاهنا هو مجرَّدُ الاستعانة، لا ما هو المتعارف من أنه يكون المال للمبضع، والعمل من الآخر، ولما صح استعانة المضارب بالأجنبي; فلأن يصح استعانته برب المال، وهو أشفق عليه كان أولى. انتهى. فلذا فسره القُهُسْتانِيُّ بقوله: أي يستعين المضارب بأحد في التجارة. كما في «النهاية»، ولو كان المستعانُ ربُّ المالِ فيبيع ويشتري للمضارب. انتهى. [٢]قوله: خلافا لزفر; فإنّ عنده تفسد المضاربة بإبضاعه ربِّ المال; لأنَّ رَبَّ المال حينئذ متصرف لنفسه، وهو لا يصلح أن يكون وكيلاً فيه، فيكون مسترداً. ولنا: إنَّ التصرف في المضاربة لما كان حقاً للمضارب فرب المال يصلح أن يكون وكيلاً عنه في التصرف فيه; فلذا قالوا: إنَّ المضارب لو أمرَ رَبُّ المالِ أن يبيع أو يشتري له جاز.
[1] His saying: 'And he gives as bida'ah...' etc.; meaning it is permissible for the mudarib in absolute mudarabah to give the capital as bida'ah for trade, even if the mudarib gives it to the capital owner. Al-ibda' is that the capital belongs to the one giving it (al-mubdi'), and the work is from another, and all profit is for the capital owner. Al-Birjandi said: The meaning of ibda' here is merely seeking assistance, not the conventional meaning that the capital is for the one giving it and the work from another; and since the mudarib's seeking assistance from a stranger is valid, then his seeking assistance from the capital owner, who is more compassionate, is even more appropriate. End. Hence al-Qahustani interpreted it by saying: The mudarib seeks assistance from someone in trade, as in al-Nihayah, even if the one sought for assistance is the capital owner, so he sells and buys for the mudarib. End. [2] His saying: 'Contrary to Zufar'; for according to him, the mudarabah is invalidated by his giving bida'ah to the capital owner, because then the capital owner is disposing for himself, and he is not suitable to be an agent in it, so it becomes a revocation. Our proof: Since the right of disposal in mudarabah belongs to the mudarib, the capital owner is suitable to be his agent in disposal, therefore they said: If the mudarib orders the capital owner to sell or buy for him, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage addresses the mudarib's right to give the capital as bida'ah (ibda'). Bida'ah is a transaction where the capital owner provides funds and another person trades, with all profit to the capital owner. The mudarib is permitted to do this even with the capital owner himself, and it does not nullify the mudarabah. Zufar held that it does nullify because the capital owner would be acting for himself and cannot be the mudarib's agent. The Hanafi response: since the mudarib has the right to trade, the capital owner can act as his agent in that trade; therefore it is valid. The definition of ibda' here is simply seeking assistance, not the technical bida'ah contract.

Key Terms
إبضاع (ibḍā‘): Giving capital as bida'ah; here defined as merely seeking assistance in trade, not the full bida'ah contract.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a Hanafi scholar known for his independent views often differing from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The mudarabah remains valid, and the capital owner acts as the mudarib's agent.
C) The profit becomes entirely the capital owner's.
D) The mudarib becomes liable for the capital.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the mudarib to give capital as bida'ah to the capital owner as a form of assistance, and this does not invalidate the mudarabah.

Mudarib's Authority in Absolute Mudarabah: Deposit, Pledge, and Hire
Ruling on mudarib's customary trade actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the mudarib in an absolute mudarabah may perform customary trade practices and apply it to determine permissibility in new scenarios.

Core Idea
In absolute mudarabah, mudarib's authority includes all customary trade practices.

The passage clarifies that in an unrestricted (absolute) mudarabah, the mudarib does not need explicit permission to deposit, pledge, take pledges, or hire workers. These actions are allowed because they are standard practices of merchants in their trade, and the contract's objective – profit through trade – necessitates them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Actions
List the four actions mentioned: deposit (īdā‘), pledge (rahn), taking pledge (irtihān), and hiring (ījār).
These are all transactions that facilitate trade.
2
Recognize the Legal Basis
The justification is that these actions are 'min ṣanī‘ al-tujjār' – from the practices of merchants. The mudarib's authority is interpreted broadly to include whatever is customary in trade.
Custom ('urf) plays a key role in determining scope.
3
Link to Contract Type
The phrase 'wa al-‘aqd muṭlaq' (and the contract is absolute) indicates that this is an unrestricted mudarabah. In a restricted mudarabah, the mudarib would be limited to specified activities.
Absolute mudarabah grants wide discretion.
4
Apply the Principle
If a new practice becomes standard in a particular trade (e.g., using digital payment platforms), it would also be included. Conversely, actions that are not customary would require explicit permission.
The custom of the specific trade is relevant.
5
Distinguish Restricted Mudarabah
If the rabb al-mal restricts the mudarib (e.g., 'trade only in wheat'), then deposit, pledge, etc. are allowed only if they are necessary for that specific trade or part of the agreement.
Restrictions must be stated at contract formation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣) قوله: ويودع ...الخ; فإن هذا كله من صنيع التجار في تجارتهم، والعقد مطلق، ولا يحصل المقصود منه، وهو الربح إلا بالتجارة، فيتناول ما هو من صنع التجار في تجارتهم.
[3] His saying: 'And he deposits...' etc.; for all of this is from the practices of merchants in their trade, and the contract is absolute, and its objective, which is profit, is only achieved through trade, so it includes what is from the practice of merchants in their trade.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage explains that in an unrestricted mudarabah (mutaqah), the mudarib may deposit the capital, give it as collateral, take collateral, and hire workers, because these are all standard practices of merchants in their trade. Since the objective of mudarabah is profit through trade, and trade necessarily involves these activities, they are included by default.

Key Terms
إيداع (īdā‘): Deposit; entrusting property to another for safekeeping.
رهن (rahn): Pledge; giving property as security for a debt.
ارتهان (irtihān): Taking a pledge; accepting property as security.
إيجار (ījār): Hiring; leasing someone's services or property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because these are standard trade practices necessary to achieve profit.
C) Because the mudarib owns the capital.
D) Because the capital owner's permission is presumed.

Key Takeaway
In an absolute mudarabah, the mudarib's authority extends to all customary trade practices, including deposit, pledge, taking pledges, and hiring, without need for explicit permission.

The Mudarib's Permissible Trade Acts in Unrestricted Mudarabah
Ruling on permissible acts and dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts permissible for a mudarib in an unrestricted mudarabah and explain the Hanafi proof for their permissibility, including the dispute with Zufar regarding ibda' to the capital owner.

Core Idea
The mudarib's authority includes all customary trade acts; the capital owner can even be his agent.

In an unrestricted mudarabah, the mudarib is empowered to carry out various trade-related acts to achieve profit. This lesson explores the specific acts that are permissible, such as giving the capital as bida'ah, depositing, pledging, and accepting hawala. We will also examine the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar concerning one of these acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of permissible acts
Read the opening of the passage to list the acts the mudarib may do: give as bida'ah (even to the capital owner), deposit, pledge, borrow, hire, and accept hawala.
These are all customary in trade.
2
Understand the basis: custom
The passage states these are 'from the practice of merchants in their trade.' The unrestricted contract implies all such acts are included.
Istihsan based on custom (urf tijari).
3
Analyze the specific act of ibda' and the Zufar dispute
Focus on the ruling that the mudarib may give capital as bida'ah even to the capital owner. Zufar holds this corrupts the contract. The Hanafi majority allows it.
The key difference: Zufar sees it as owner acting for himself; Hanafis see it as agency.
4
Trace the Hanafi proof
The mudarib has the right of disposition (haqq al-tasarruf), so the capital owner can be his agent. This is from istihsan and customary practice.
The dalil is aqli (rational) based on ownership of rights.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
If the owner gives bida'ah according to Zufar, the mudarabah is invalid; according to the majority, it remains valid.
Practical consequence for validity of the contract.

ويبضع ولو رب المال ولا تفسد هي به، ويودع، ويَرْهَن، ويَرْتَهِن، ويُؤَجر ويبضع ولو ربِّ المالِ ولا تفسد هي به : أي لا تفسد المضاربة بأن يُبضع رَبُّ المال خلافا لزُفَرَ"، ويودع"، ويَرْهَن، ويَرْتَهن، ويُؤَجر ه ( والجواب عن هذه الرواية : إنَّه تعريض على الهلاك غير مسلم، فإنَّ الظاهر فيه السلامة، والموهوم لا يعتبر، ألا ترى أنَّ للمودع أن يسافر بالوديعة مع أنه ليس له أن يودع ، والمضارب له ذلك، فكان أولى منها، وأولى بالجواز. وفي فتاوى قاضي خان» : روى أبو يوسف له عن أبي حنيفة له : إنه لا يسافر به أصلا. انتهى. فظهر أنَّ ما ذكره الشارحُ الله بقوله : ليس له أن يسافر، وبقوله : عند أبي حنيفة له : إن دفع إلى آخره رواية عنهما ، لا مذهبهما كما أفاده لفظ : عند ؛ فإنّه لا يذكر إلا للمذهب ، فكان المناسب لفظة: عن ؛ لا لفظ : عند ؛ فتأمل. [۱] قوله : ويبضع.... الخ ؛ يعني وجاز للمضارب في المضاربة المطلقة أن يعطي المال بضاعة للتجارة، ولو أعطاه المضارب ربَّ المال، والإبضاعُ : هو أن يكون المال للمبضع، والعمل من آخر، والربح كله لرب المال. وقال البرْجُنْدِي : والمراد بالإيضاع هاهنا هو مجرَّدُ الاستعانة، لا ما هو المتعارف من أنه يكون المال للمبضع ، والعمل من الآخر ، ولما صح استعانة المضارب بالأجنبي ؛ فلأن يصح استعانته برب المال، وهو أشفق عليه كان أولى. انتهى. فلذا فسره القُهُسْتانِيُّ بقوله : أي يستعين المضارب بأحد في التجارة. كما في «النهاية»، ولو كان المستعانُ ربُّ المالِ فيبيع ويشتري للمضارب. انتهى. [٢]قوله : خلافا لزفر ؛ فإنّ عنده تفسد المضاربة بإبضاعه ربِّ المال ؛ لأنَّ رَبَّ المال حينئذ متصرف لنفسه، وهو لا يصلح أن يكون وكيلاً فيه، فيكون مسترداً. ولنا : إنَّ التصرف في المضاربة لما كان حقاً للمضارب فرب المال يصلح أن يكون وكيلاً . عنه في التصرف فيه ؛ فلذا قالوا : إنَّ المضارب لو أمرَ رَبُّ المالِ أن يبيع أو يشتري له جاز. [٣) قوله : ويودع ...الخ ؛ فإن هذا كله من صنيع التجار في تجارتهم، والعقد مطلق، ولا يحصل المقصود منه، وهو الربح إلا بالتجارة، فيتناول ما هو من صنع التجار في تجارتهم. ويستأجر ، ويحتالُ بالثَّمن على الأيسر والأعسر، وليس له أن يضارب إلا بأذن المالك، أو باعمل برأيك ويستأجر ، ويحتالُ بالثَّمن" على الأيسر والأعسر ) : أي يقبلُ الحوالة". (وليس له أن يضارب إلا بأذن المالك ، أو باعمل برأيك)، الضابط أن [٤] الشيء لا يَتَضَمَّنُ مثله بل يَتَضَمَّنُ دونَه كالإيداع، ونحوه [١] قوله : ويستأجر ؛ فلو استأجر أرضاً بيضاء ليزرعها أو يغرسها جاز، صرح به في «الظهيرية»، وغيرها. [٢] قوله : ويحتالُ بالثمن ؛ إذ الحوالة من عادة التجار، ولا يقاس ذلك على الوصي، حيث لا يجوز له أن يحتال بالثمن من مالِ الصغير على الأعسر ؛ لأنَّ تصرّف الوصي مبني على النظر، وتصرُّف المضارب مبني على صنيع التجار. [٣] قوله : أي يقبل الحوالة ؛ قبولُ الحوالة ليس معنى الاحتيال ؛ لأن الاحتيال كونه محتالاً، ، وذلك برضاء المحيل والمحال عليه والمحال، لكنَّ الشارح له اقتصر عليه ؛ لأنه هو المقصود هاهنا. [٤]قوله : وليس له أن يضارب ... الخ ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يضارب مال المضاربة لآخر إلا بإذن رب المال صريحاً ، أو لقوله للمضارب : اعمل برأيك ؛ لأنَّ الشيء لا يتضمَّنُ مثله ، فلا بد من التنصيص عليه، أو التفويض المطلق إليه. كما أن الوكيل لا يملك التوكيل إلا لقول الأصيل : اعمل برأيك ، بخلاف الإيضاع والإيداع ؛ لأنهما دون المضاربة لا مثلها فتتضمنهما، فالقاعدة الكلية أن الشيء لا يتضمَّنُ مثله ؛ فالمضاربة لا تتضمَّنُ المضاربة الأخرى، ويتضمن دونه ؛ فيتضمن المتضارية الإيداع وما أشبه ذلك. واعلم أن التصرفات في المضاربة على ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : ما هو من باب المضاربة وتوابعها ، وهو الإيداع، والإبضاع، والإجارة، والاستئجار ،والرهن والارتهان ، وما أشبه ذلك، فيملكها المضارب بمطلق عقدِ المضاربة. والثاني : ما هو ليس من باب المضاربة المطلقة، لكن يصلح أن يلحق بها عند وجود الدلالة، وهو إثبات الشركة في المضاربة بأن يدفع إلى غيره مضاربةً، أو يخلط مال
And he may give as bida'ah even to the owner of the capital and the mudarabah is not corrupted by it, and he may deposit, and may pledge, and may borrow, and may hire. 'And he may give as bida'ah even to the owner of the capital and it is not corrupted by it': meaning the mudarabah is not corrupted by the owner of the capital giving as bida'ah, contrary to Zufar. And he may deposit, and may pledge, and may borrow, and may hire. (And the answer to this narration: it is an exposure to destruction which is not accepted, for the apparent is safety, and the conjectural is not considered. Do you not see that the depositor may travel with the deposit while he may not deposit, and the mudarib has that, so it is more deserving than it, and more deserving of permissibility. And in Fatawa Qadi Khan: Abu Yusuf related from Abu Hanifa: he does not travel with it at all. End. So it appears that what the commentator mentioned by his saying 'he may not travel', and by his saying 'according to Abu Hanifa: if he gives...' to the end, is a narration from them, not their madhhab, as indicated by the word 'according to' (عند); for it is only mentioned for the madhhab, so the appropriate word would be 'from' (عن), not 'according to' (عند); so reflect. [1] His saying: 'and he may give as bida'ah...' etc.; meaning it is permissible for the mudarib in unrestricted mudarabah to give the capital as bida'ah for trade, even if the mudarib gives it to the owner of the capital. And al-bida'ah is: that the capital belongs to the one who gives as bida'ah, and the work from another, and all profit belongs to the owner of the capital. And al-Barjandi said: the intended meaning of bida'ah here is mere assistance, not the customary meaning that the capital belongs to the one who gives as bida'ah and the work from another. And since the mudarib's seeking assistance from a stranger is valid, then that his seeking assistance from the owner of the capital, who is more compassionate towards it, is more appropriate. End. So al-Quhistani interpreted it by saying: meaning the mudarib seeks assistance from someone in trade. As in al-Nihayah, even if the one sought for assistance is the owner of the capital, so he sells and buys for the mudarib. End. [2] His saying: 'contrary to Zufar'; for according to him the mudarabah is corrupted by the mudarib giving as bida'ah to the owner of the capital; because then the owner of the capital is acting for himself, and he is not fit to be an agent in it, so he is considered a retractor. And for us: since the disposition in mudarabah is a right of the mudarib, the owner of the capital is fit to be an agent for him in the disposition; therefore they said: if the mudarib orders the owner of the capital to sell or buy for him, it is permissible. [3] His saying: 'and he may deposit...' etc.; for all this is from the practice of merchants in their trade, and the contract is unrestricted, and its objective, which is profit, is not attained except through trade, so it includes that which is from the practice of merchants in their trade. And he may hire, and may accept transfer of debt (hawala) regarding the price whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent. And he may not sub-contract mudarabah except with the permission of the owner, or if he says 'act according to your opinion'. And he may hire, and may accept transfer of debt (hawala) regarding the price whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent: meaning he accepts hawala. (And he may not sub-contract mudarabah except with the permission of the owner, or if he says 'act according to your opinion'), the principle is that a thing does not include its like but includes what is beneath it, like deposit and the like. [1] His saying: 'and he may hire'; so if he hires a white land to cultivate or plant it, it is permissible, as stated in al-Zahiriyyah and others. [2] His saying: 'and he may accept hawala regarding the price'; because hawala is from the custom of merchants, and it is not analogous to the executor (wasi), where he may not accept hawala regarding the price of the minor's property from an insolvent debtor because the executor's disposition is based on consideration, and the mudarib's disposition is based on the practice of merchants. [3] His saying: 'meaning he accepts hawala'; accepting hawala is not the meaning of ihtiyal (transferring the debt), because ihtiyal is being the transferee (muhtal), and that requires the consent of the transferor (muhil), the transferee (muhtal), and the debtor (muhal 'alayh), but the commentator limited it to that because it is the intended meaning here. [4] His saying: 'and he may not sub-contract mudarabah...' etc.; meaning the mudarib may not give the mudarabah capital to another as mudarabah except with explicit permission from the owner of the capital, or if he says to the mudarib 'act according to your opinion'; because a thing does not include its like, so specification or absolute delegation is necessary. Just as an agent does not have the power to appoint a sub-agent except by the principal saying 'act according to your opinion', unlike bida'ah and deposit because they are below mudarabah, not like it, so it includes them. So the general rule is that a thing does not include its like; thus mudarabah does not include another mudarabah, but includes what is beneath it; so mudarabah includes deposit and the like. Know that the dispositions in mudarabah are of three types: First: that which is from the chapter of mudarabah and its ancillaries, which are deposit, bida'ah, hiring, renting, pledging, borrowing, and the like; the mudarib owns these by the absolute contract of mudarabah. Second: that which is not from the chapter of unrestricted mudarabah, but may be attached to it when there is an indication, which is establishing partnership in mudarabah by giving to another as mudarabah, or mixing capital
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the powers of the mudarib (agent) in an unrestricted mudarabah (qirad). The author begins by listing permissible acts: the mudarib may give the capital as bida'ah (putting out for trade) even to the owner of the capital himself, and this does not corrupt the mudarabah. This is contrary to Zufar, who held that such an act renders the contract void because the owner becomes a self-dealer. The Hanafi response argues that since the mudarib has the right of disposition, the owner can act as his agent. The passage then extends the list to include depositing, pledging, borrowing, hiring, and accepting hawala (transfer of debt) whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent. All these are customary trade practices and thus permitted in an unrestricted contract. However, sub-contracting the mudarabah to another person is not allowed without explicit permission from the capital owner or if the owner told the mudarib to 'act according to your opinion'. The principle is that a contract includes only acts that are lesser or similar in nature, but not its exact equivalent; hence mudarabah does not imply another mudarabah. The commentary concludes by classifying dispositions into three categories: those directly part of mudarabah (e.g., deposit, bida'ah, hire), those that may be attached with evidence (e.g., partnership), and presumably a third category not fully shown. The Hanafi position is that the mudarib's authority is broad but limited by the contract's scope and the owner's explicit restrictions.

Key Terms
إبضاع (ibḍāʿ): Giving capital as bida'ah for trade, where the capital belongs to the giver, work from another, and all profit to the capital owner; here it is interpreted as mere assistance.
حوالة (ḥawālah): Transfer of debt; the mudarib may accept transfer of the price of goods from a debtor, whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent.
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A contract of partnership in profit where one party provides capital and the other provides labor (also called qirad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the mudarib giving the capital as bida'ah to the capital owner?
A) It is permissible and does not corrupt the mudarabah.
B) It is permissible but corrupts the mudarabah.
C) It is prohibited and invalidates the contract.
D) It is only allowed if the owner gives specific permission.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib in an unrestricted mudarabah may perform all customary trade acts, including involving the capital owner as an agent, without corrupting the contract.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
6 min
The Mudarib's Limitations: Sub-Contracting and the 'Does Not Include Its Like' Principle
Ruling on sub-contracting and the legal maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the restriction on sub-contracting mudarabah and apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'a thing does not include its like' to distinguish between permissible ancillaries and prohibited equivalents.

Core Idea
A mudarib may not sub-contract the capital to another mudarib, because mudarabah does not include its like.

While the mudarib has broad authority, there is a critical limitation: he cannot give the capital to another person as mudarabah without the owner's explicit permission. This is based on the principle that a contract includes acts inferior to it but not its equivalent. This lesson examines that restriction and classifies the mudarib's dispositions into three categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the restricted act
The passage states: 'وليس له أن يضارب إلا بأذن المالك أو باعمل برأيك' meaning sub-contracting mudarabah to another is prohibited without permission or absolute delegation.
This is a key limitation.
2
Understand the legal principle
The principle is: 'الشيء لا يتضمن مثله' (a thing does not include its like). Mudarabah does not include another mudarabah because they are equal in nature.
But it includes lesser acts like deposit and bida'ah.
3
Apply to the classification of acts
The passage classifies disposition into three categories: 1) acts directly part of mudarabah (deposit, bida'ah, hire, etc.), 2) acts that may be attached with evidence (partnership by mixing capital), 3) acts that are like mudarabah (sub-contracting) which require permission.
The third category is prohibited without permission.
4
Compare with other scenarios
Analogize: an agent (wakil) cannot appoint a sub-agent unless authorized. Similarly, a mudarib cannot sub-contract unless the owner says 'اعمل برأيك'.
This is consistent with agency principles.
5
Identify the difference between sub-contracting and lesser acts
Why is deposit allowed but sub-contracting not? Because deposit is a lower type of fiduciary relationship, while mudarabah is a higher/equal one. The contract includes its subordinates but not its peers.
This is the core of the maxim.

Do you not see that the depositor may travel with the deposit while he may not deposit, and the mudarib has that, so it is more deserving than it, and more deserving of permissibility. And in Fatawa Qadi Khan: Abu Yusuf related from Abu Hanifa: he does not travel with it at all. End. So it appears that what the commentator mentioned by his saying 'he may not travel', and by his saying 'according to Abu Hanifa: if he gives...' to the end, is a narration from them, not their madhhab, as indicated by the word 'according to' (عند); for it is only mentioned for the madhhab, so the appropriate word would be 'from' (عن), not 'according to' (عند); so reflect. [1] His saying: 'and he may give as bida'ah...' etc.; meaning it is permissible for the mudarib in unrestricted mudarabah to give the capital as bida'ah for trade, even if the mudarib gives it to the owner of the capital. And al-bida'ah is: that the capital belongs to the one who gives as bida'ah, and the work from another, and all profit belongs to the owner of the capital. And al-Barjandi said: the intended meaning of bida'ah here is mere assistance, not the customary meaning that the capital belongs to the one who gives as bida'ah and the work from another. And since the mudarib's seeking assistance from a stranger is valid, then that his seeking assistance from the owner of the capital, who is more compassionate towards it, is more appropriate. End. So al-Quhistani interpreted it by saying: meaning the mudarib seeks assistance from someone in trade. As in al-Nihayah, even if the one sought for assistance is the owner of the capital, so he sells and buys for the mudarib. End. [2] His saying: 'contrary to Zufar'; for according to him the mudarabah is corrupted by the mudarib giving as bida'ah to the owner of the capital; because then the owner of the capital is acting for himself, and he is not fit to be an agent in it, so he is considered a retractor. And for us: since the disposition in mudarabah is a right of the mudarib, the owner of the capital is fit to be an agent for him in the disposition; therefore they said: if the mudarib orders the owner of the capital to sell or buy for him, it is permissible. [3] His saying: 'and he may deposit...' etc.; for all this is from the practice of merchants in their trade, and the contract is unrestricted, and its objective, which is profit, is not attained except through trade, so it includes that which is from the practice of merchants in their trade. And he may hire, and may accept transfer of debt (hawala) regarding the price whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent. And he may not sub-contract mudarabah except with the permission of the owner, or if he says 'act according to your opinion'. And he may hire, and may accept transfer of debt (hawala) regarding the price whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent: meaning he accepts hawala. (And he may not sub-contract mudarabah except with the permission of the owner, or if he says 'act according to your opinion'), the principle is that a thing does not include its like but includes what is beneath it, like deposit and the like. [1] His saying: 'and he may hire'; so if he hires a white land to cultivate or plant it, it is permissible, as stated in al-Zahiriyyah and others. [2] His saying: 'and he may accept hawala regarding the price'; because hawala is from the custom of merchants, and it is not analogous to the executor (wasi), where he may not accept hawala regarding the price of the minor's property from an insolvent debtor because the executor's disposition is based on consideration, and the mudarib's disposition is based on the practice of merchants. [3] His saying: 'meaning he accepts hawala'; accepting hawala is not the meaning of ihtiyal (transferring the debt), because ihtiyal is being the transferee (muhtal), and that requires the consent of the transferor (muhil), the transferee (muhtal), and the debtor (muhal 'alayh), but the commentator limited it to that because it is the intended meaning here. [4] His saying: 'and he may not sub-contract mudarabah...' etc.; meaning the mudarib may not give the mudarabah capital to another as mudarabah except with explicit permission from the owner of the capital, or if he says to the mudarib 'act according to your opinion'; because a thing does not include its like, so specification or absolute delegation is necessary. Just as an agent does not have the power to appoint a sub-agent except by the principal saying 'act according to your opinion', unlike bida'ah and deposit because they are below mudarabah, not like it, so it includes them. So the general rule is that a thing does not include its like; thus mudarabah does not include another mudarabah, but includes what is beneath it; so mudarabah includes deposit and the like. Know that the dispositions in mudarabah are of three types: First: that which is from the chapter of mudarabah and its ancillaries, which are deposit, bida'ah, hiring, renting, pledging, borrowing, and the like; the mudarib owns these by the absolute contract of mudarabah. Second: that which is not from the chapter of unrestricted mudarabah, but may be attached to it when there is an indication, which is establishing partnership in mudarabah by giving to another as mudarabah, or mixing capital
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the powers of the mudarib (agent) in an unrestricted mudarabah (qirad). The author begins by listing permissible acts: the mudarib may give the capital as bida'ah (putting out for trade) even to the owner of the capital himself, and this does not corrupt the mudarabah. This is contrary to Zufar, who held that such an act renders the contract void because the owner becomes a self-dealer. The Hanafi response argues that since the mudarib has the right of disposition, the owner can act as his agent. The passage then extends the list to include depositing, pledging, borrowing, hiring, and accepting hawala (transfer of debt) whether the debtor is wealthy or insolvent. All these are customary trade practices and thus permitted in an unrestricted contract. However, sub-contracting the mudarabah to another person is not allowed without explicit permission from the capital owner or if the owner told the mudarib to 'act according to your opinion'. The principle is that a contract includes only acts that are lesser or similar in nature, but not its exact equivalent; hence mudarabah does not imply another mudarabah. The commentary concludes by classifying dispositions into three categories: those directly part of mudarabah (e.g., deposit, bida'ah, hire), those that may be attached with evidence (e.g., partnership), and presumably a third category not fully shown. The Hanafi position is that the mudarib's authority is broad but limited by the contract's scope and the owner's explicit restrictions.

Key Terms
إيداع (īdāʿ): Deposit; giving property to another for safekeeping, without permission to use.
قاعدة: الشيء لا يتضمن مثله (qāʿidah: al-shay' lā yatadammanu mithlahu): Legal maxim: a thing does not include its like; a contract does not authorize another identical contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal basis for prohibiting the mudarib from sub-contracting mudarabah without permission?
A) The owner's right to know who manages his capital.
B) The principle that a thing does not include its like.
C) The fact that sub-contracting is not customary in trade.
D) The requirement that all contracts be verbal.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib cannot sub-contract the capital to another mudarib without explicit permission or absolute delegation, because a contract does not include its equal.

The Mudārib's Authority to Hire and Accept Ḥawāla
Ruling and commentary on mudarib hiring and hawala acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mudārib's authority to hire and accept ḥawāla, explain why it differs from the waṣī's limitations, and apply the ruling to a hiring scenario.

Core Idea
The mudārib acts as a trader, not a guardian — so he can hire and accept ḥawāla.

In mudāraba, the mudārib manages capital and engages in trade. This lesson examines two specific authorities: hiring (e.g., leasing land) and accepting a transfer of debt (ḥawāla). The commentary contrasts these with the stricter limits on a waṣī (guardian of an orphan), highlighting that the mudārib's authority is rooted in trade practice, not mere preservation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states: 'He can hire and he can accept transfer of debt (ḥawāla) from the solvent and the insolvent.' This is a permission, not an obligation.
2
Understand the First Authority (Hiring)
The commentator explains that hiring barren land to cultivate or plant trees is permissible. This is an expansion of the mudārib's authority to include land development.
The source cites al-Ẓahīriyya as authority.
3
Understand the Second Authority (Hawala)
Accepting ḥawāla is permissible because it is a common trade custom. The mudārib's authority is based on trade practice (ṣanīʿ al-tujjār).
4
Contrast with the Wasī (Guardian)
The waṣī cannot accept ḥawāla from an insolvent debtor because his role is preservation (naẓar), not active trade. This highlights the mudārib's broader latitude.
5
Clarify the Term 'Iḥtiyāl'
In this context, 'iḥtiyāl' means accepting ḥawāla (being the transferee), not trickery. The commentator clarifies this to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستأجر ، ويحتالُ بالثَّمن على الأيسر والأعسر ) : أي يقبل الحوالة. [١] قوله : ويستأجر ؛ فلو استأجر أرضاً بيضاء ليزرعها أو يغرسها جاز، صرح به في «الظهيرية»، وغيرها. [٢] قوله : ويحتالُ بالثمن ؛ إذ الحوالة من عادة التجار، ولا يقاس ذلك على الوصي، حيث لا يجوز له أن يحتال بالثمن من مالِ الصغير على الأعسر ؛ لأنَّ تصرّف الوصي مبني على النظر، وتصرُّف المضارب مبني على صنيع التجار. [٣] قوله : أي يقبل الحوالة ؛ قبولُ الحوالة ليس معنى الاحتيال ؛ لأن الاحتيال كونه محتالاً، ، وذلك برضاء المحيل والمحال عليه والمحال، لكنَّ الشارح له اقتصر عليه ؛ لأنه هو المقصود هاهنا.
And he can hire, and he can accept transfer of debt (ḥawāla) from the solvent and the insolvent: i.e. he accepts the ḥawāla. [1] His words: 'and he can hire' – so if he hires barren land to cultivate or plant trees, it is permissible, as stated in al-Ẓahīriyya and others. [2] His words: 'and he can accept transfer of debt' – because ḥawāla is the custom of traders, and this cannot be analogized to the guardian, who is not permitted to accept transfer of debt from the property of a minor from an insolvent person; because the guardian's actions are based on caution, while the mudārib's actions are based on the practice of traders. [3] His words: 'i.e. he accepts ḥawāla' – accepting ḥawāla is not the meaning of 'iḥtiyāl' (trickery); because 'iḥtiyāl' means being the creditor, and that requires the consent of the transferor, the transferee, and the transferred. But the commentator restricted it to this because that is what is intended here.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling that the mudārib (manager of a mudāraba partnership) has the authority to hire (e.g. land for cultivation) and to accept a ḥawāla (transfer of debt). The commentary clarifies the scope: hiring is permissible even for barren land to be developed. Accepting ḥawāla is permissible because it is a common trade practice. The commentary contrasts this with a wasī (guardian of an orphan), who cannot accept a ḥawāla from an insolvent debtor because the guardian's duty is strict preservation, whereas the mudārib's role is active trade. Finally, it clarifies that 'accepting ḥawāla' here means being the transferee (muḥtāl), not the original creditor.

Key Terms
الاحتيال (al-iḥtiyāl): In this context, 'accepting a ḥawāla' (being the transferee). Not deceit or trickery.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The legal guardian of an orphan's property, whose actions are strictly for preservation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the mudārib has explicit permission in the contract
D) Because the waṣī is prohibited from all debts

Key Takeaway
The mudārib can hire and accept ḥawāla because these are trade practices, unlike the wasī who must preserve capital.

Mudārib Actions: The Three Categories and Lending/Borrowing Prohibition
Classification of mudārib actions into three categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify mudārib actions into three categories and apply the rule that lending and borrowing are prohibited without explicit permission.

Core Idea
Lending and borrowing are never automatic in mudāraba—they require explicit permission because they are not trade.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from the Hanafi fiqh literature that classifies all actions a mudārib may take. It distinguishes between actions intrinsic to trade (automatically permitted), those that can be annexed with indication, and those that are entirely separate—lending and borrowing. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying the mudāraba contract and avoiding invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage divides mudārib actions into three types. List them as described in the text.
First: trade accessories (tawābi'). Second: actions requiring indication (e.g., partnership mixing). Third: actions never annexed (lending/borrowing).
2
Understand automatic permissions
The first category includes actions like deposit, hiring, mortgage, etc. These are automatically permitted because they are accessories to trade.
The mudārib can do these without any special permission.
3
Analyze the second category
Actions like mixing capital or subcontracting are not automatically allowed but can be annexed if there is an indication (e.g., owner says 'act on your opinion').
General permission can cover these if the owner's words imply it.
4
Understand prohibition of lending/borrowing
Lending and borrowing are not trade and cannot be annexed. Even 'act on your opinion' does not permit them. They require explicit mention.
Lending is a donation; borrowing encumbers the owner's liability.
5
Apply to borrowing scenarios
Distinguish when a purchase is borrowing: buying on credit after capital is exhausted. Buying with a different currency is not borrowing because all currencies are one genus.
This nuance is important for practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وليس له أن يضارب إلا بأذن المالك ، أو باعمل برأيك)، الضابط أن الشيء لا يَتَضَمَّنُ مثله بل يَتَضَمَّنُ دونَه كالإيداع، ونحوه. واعلم أن التصرفات في المضاربة على ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : ما هو من باب المضاربة وتوابعها ، وهو الإيداع، والإبضاع، والإجارة، والاستئجار ،والرهن والارتهان ، وما أشبه ذلك، فيملكها المضارب بمطلق عقدِ المضاربة. والثاني : ما هو ليس من باب المضاربة المطلقة، لكن يصلح أن يلحق بها عند وجود الدلالة، وهو إثبات الشركة في المضاربة بأن يدفع إلى غيره مضاربةً، أو يخلط مال المضاربة بماله ، أو بمال غيره. فالمضارب لا يملكه بمطلق المضاربة؛ لأنَّ ربِّ المال لم يرض بشركة غيره، وهو أمر زائد على ما تقوم به التجارة، فلا يتناولُهُ مطلق عقد المضاربة، لكن يصلح أن يلحقها بالتعميم. والثالث : ما لا يمكن أن يلحق بالمضاربة وهو الإقراض والاستدانة على المال، فإنَّ الإقراض ليس بتجارة، وكذا الاستدانة على المال، بل تصرف بغير رأس المال ، والتوكيلُ مقيد برأس المال، فالمضاربُ لا يملكه، وإن قال له ربِّ المال : اعمل برأيك ؛ لعدم إمكان لحوقه المضاربة، فلا يتناوله قوله : اعمل برأيك. [1] قوله : ولا أن يقرض... الخ ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يقرض أو يستقرض ، وإن قال له المالك : اعمل برأيك ، إلا إذا نص عليهما فيجوزان له ، أما الإقراض ؛ فلانه تبرع ليس من ضرورات التجارة، فلا يملكه المضارب، وإن قيل له : اعمل برأيك، كما لا يملك الهبة والصدقة. وأما الاستدانة ؛ فلأنَّ فيها شغلُ ذمَّةِ المالك، وأما إذا نصَّ عليهما ؛ فلأنَّ المنع من حق المالك، وله تركه، فإن استدان بعد الإذن فالمشترى بينهما نصفان على أنه شركة وجوه لا مضاربة، ذكره الشمني ، وغيره. وصورة الاستدانة على ما في شرح الوافي»: أن يشتري شيئا بالدراهم أو الدنانير بعدما اشترى برأس المال سلعة ، أو يشتري بمكيل أو موزون ورأس المال في يده دنانير أو دراهم، فإنه اشترى بغير رأس المال، فكان استدانة. بخلاف ما لو اشترى بدراهم ورأس المال في يده دنانير، أو بدنانير ورأس المال في یده دراهم، فإنَّ الدراهم والدنانير جنس واحدٌ في الثمنية، فلا يكون هذا شراء بدين. وقال العلامة الأقطع في «شرح مختصر القدوري»: لا يجوز للمضارب أن يستدين على المضاربة، وإن فعل ذلك لم يجز على ربِّ المال، ألا ترى أنه إذا اشترى برأس المال
His words: 'And he may not enter into another mudāraba except with the permission of the owner, or if he said: act on your opinion.' The principle is that a thing does not imply its like, but it does imply what is less than it, like deposit and similar. Know that the actions in mudāraba are of three types: First: those that belong to the domain of mudāraba and its accessories, such as deposit, consignment, hiring, renting, mortgage, and similar; these the mudārib owns by the absolute contract of mudāraba. Second: those that are not from the domain of absolute mudāraba but can be annexed to it when there is an indication, such as establishing partnership in the mudāraba by giving to another as mudāraba or mixing the mudāraba capital with his own property or another's property. The mudārib does not own this by absolute mudāraba because the owner has not consented to another's partnership, and it is additional to what trade requires, so the absolute mudāraba contract does not include it, but it can be annexed by generalization. Third: what cannot be annexed to mudāraba, which is lending and borrowing on the capital. Because lending is not trade, and likewise borrowing on the capital is a transaction without the capital, and the agency (tawakkul) is tied to the capital, so the mudārib does not own it, even if the owner says to him 'act on your opinion', because it cannot be annexed to mudāraba, so his statement 'act on your opinion' does not include it. [1] His words: 'And he may not lend or borrow...' i.e. the mudārib may not lend or borrow, even if the owner says 'act on your opinion', unless he explicitly mentions them, then they are permissible for him. As for lending, it is a donation not necessary for trade, so the mudārib does not own it even if told 'act on your opinion', just as he does not own giving gifts or charity. As for borrowing, it involves encumbering the owner's liability. And if he explicitly mentions them, the prohibition is from the owner's right, and he may waive it. If he borrows after permission, the purchased item is half and half between them on the basis of a partnership of faces (shirkat wujūh), not mudāraba; mentioned by al-Shumnī and others. The form of borrowing, according to what is in Sharḥ al-Wāfī: that he buys something with dirhams or dinars after having bought a commodity with the capital, or he buys with a quantity or weight while the capital in his hand is dinars or dirhams, then he buys without the capital, so it is borrowing. Unlike if he buys with dirhams while the capital in his hand is dinars, or with dinars while the capital is dirhams, because dirhams and dinars are one genus in terms of being currency, so this is not purchase with debt. And the 'Allāma al-Aqṭaʿ said in Sharḥ Mukhtaṣar al-Qudūrī: It is not permissible for the mudārib to borrow on the mudāraba, and if he does so, it is not valid against the owner; do you not see that if he buys with the capital...
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the mudārib's inability to subcontract (enter another mudāraba) without permission, and then expands into a three-part classification of all mudārib actions. The first category includes core trade activities (hiring, deposit, mortgage) that are automatically permitted. The second includes partnership-mixing which can be permitted if there is indication (like the owner saying 'act on your opinion'). The third includes lending and borrowing, which are never permitted unless explicitly authorized. The commentary explains that lending is a donation not essential to trade; borrowing encumbers the owner. If borrowing is authorized, the purchased goods are shared equally as a partnership of faces, not mudāraba. The passage also clarifies the form of borrowing: buying on credit when capital is in a different form (e.g., buying a commodity on credit after capital is used? Actually the text says buying with currency when capital is in hand as a different currency is not borrowing because all currencies are one genus as thamaniyya.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-muḍāraba): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profit shared according to agreement.
توابعها (tawābiʿuhā): Actions that are accessories to mudāraba, such as deposit, mortgage, hiring, which the mudārib can do without permission.
شركة الوجوه (shirkat wujūh): A partnership based on creditworthiness where each partner contributes no capital but shares profit/loss based on their reputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because lending is a form of partnership
D) Because lending is forbidden in all partnerships

Key Takeaway
Lending and borrowing are not trade accessories—they require explicit authorization in the mudāraba contract.

Mudarib Cannot Lend or Borrow Without Explicit Authorization
Ruling on mudarib's inability to lend or borrow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on mudarib's lending and borrowing and identify the conditions under which it becomes permissible.

Core Idea
A mudarib cannot lend or borrow mudaraba capital without explicit owner authorization.

This lesson examines the boundaries of a mudarib's authority in a mudaraba contract. While the mudarib is empowered to trade, acts like lending (iqrad) and borrowing (istidana) fall outside the scope of trade and require explicit permission. We will analyze the reasoning behind this restriction and the exception when the owner stipulates otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what the mudarib is prohibited from doing. State the default hukm.
The prohibition covers both lending (iqrad) and borrowing (istidana).
2
Understand the rationale
Analyze why lending and borrowing are not permitted. Lending is a gratuitous act (tabarru') not essential to trade; borrowing encumbers the owner's liability beyond the capital.
The text compares lending to gift and charity.
3
Analyze the exception
Identify the only condition when borrowing or lending becomes permissible: explicit authorisation by the owner, either in the contract or later.
Even the phrase 'act on your opinion' does not grant this authority.
4
Apply to related cases
Consider how this rule applies to analogous instruments like suftaja (letter of credit). Taking or giving suftaja is equivalent to borrowing or lending and thus similarly restricted.
The text explicitly states that suftaja falls under the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أن يقرض، أو يستدين، وإن قيل له ذلك، ما لم ينص عليهما. فالمضارب لا يملكه بمطلق المضاربة؛ لأن رب المال لم يرض بشركة غيره، وهو أمر زائد على ما تقوم به التجارة، فلا يتناوله مطلق عقد المضاربة، لكن يصلح أن يلحقها بالتعميم. والثاني: ما لا يمكن أن يلحق بالمضاربة وهو الإقراض والاستدانة على المال، فإن الإقراض ليس بتجارة، وكذا الاستدانة على المال، بل تصرف بغير رأس المال، والتوكيل مقيد برأس المال، فالمضارب لا يملكه، وإن قال له رب المال: اعمل برأيك؛ لعدم إمكان لحوقه المضاربة، فلا يتناوله قوله: اعمل برأيك. [1] قوله: ولا أن يقرض... الخ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يقرض أو يستقرض، وإن قال له المالك: اعمل برأيك، إلا إذا نص عليهما فيجوزان له. أما الإقراض؛ فلأنه تبرع ليس من ضرورات التجارة، فلا يملكه المضارب، وإن قيل له: اعمل برأيك، كما لا يملك الهبة والصدقة. وأما الاستدانة؛ فلأن فيها شغل ذمة المالك. وأما إذا نص عليهما؛ فلأن المنع من حق المالك، وله تركه. فإن استدان بعد الإذن فالمشترى بينهما نصفان على أنه شركة وجوه لا مضاربة، ذكره الشمني وغيره. وصورة الاستدانة على ما في شرح الوافي: أن يشتري شيئا بالدراهم أو الدنانير بعدما اشترى برأس المال سلعة، أو يشتري بمكيل أو موزون ورأس المال في يده دنانير أو دراهم، فإنه اشترى بغير رأس المال، فكان استدانة. بخلاف ما لو اشترى بدراهم ورأس المال في يده دنانير، أو بدنانير ورأس المال في يده دراهم، فإن الدراهم والدنانير جنس واحد في الثمنية، فلا يكون هذا شراء بدين. وقال العلامة الأقطع في شرح مختصر القدوري: لا يجوز للمضارب أن يستدين على المضاربة، وإن فعل ذلك لم يجز على رب المال، ألا ترى أنه إذا اشترى برأس المال قبلك قبل التسليم، يرجع المضارب عليه بمثله إذا كان كذلك رب المال لم يرض أن يضمن إلا مقدار رأس المال. فلو جوزنا الاستدانة لزمه ضمان ما لم يرض به، وذلك لا يصح، وإذا لم يصح استدانته على رب المال لزمه العين خاصة، وقد قالوا: ليس للمضارب أن يأخذ سفتجة؛ لأن ذلك استدانة، وهو لا يملك الاستدانة، وكذلك لا يعطي سفتجة؛ لأن ذلك قرض، وهو لا يملك القرض، ولو قال: اعمل برأيك. انتهى بقدر الضرورة.
And he may not lend or borrow, even if he is told that, unless it is stipulated. The mudarib does not have this authority by absolute mudaraba because the owner did not agree to the partnership of another, and it is something additional to what trade is based upon, so the absolute mudaraba contract does not include it, but it can be attached to it by generalization. The second: what cannot be attached to mudaraba, which is lending and borrowing against the capital, because lending is not trade, and likewise borrowing against the capital, rather it is a disposition without the capital, and the agency is restricted to the capital, so the mudarib does not have this authority even if the owner says to him: act on your opinion, because it cannot be attached to mudaraba, so his statement 'act on your opinion' does not include it. [1] His statement: 'and he may not lend...' etc.; meaning the mudarib may not lend or borrow, even if the owner says to him: act on your opinion, unless it is stipulated, then it is permissible for him. As for lending, because it is a gratuitous act not from the necessities of trade, so the mudarib does not have the right to do it even if told: act on your opinion, just as he does not have the right to give gifts or charity. As for borrowing, because it involves encumbering the owner's liability. As for if it is stipulated, because the prohibition is for the right of the owner, and he may waive it. If he borrows after permission, the purchased item is between them in halves as a partnership of credit, not mudaraba, as mentioned by al-Shumnuni and others. The form of borrowing according to what is in Sharh al-Wafi: that he buys something with dirhams or dinars after having bought a commodity with the capital, or he buys by measure or weight while the capital is in his hands as dinars or dirhams, then he has bought without the capital, so it was borrowing. Unlike if he buys with dirhams while the capital is in his hands as dinars, or with dinars while the capital is in his hands as dirhams, because dirhams and dinars are one genus in terms of being a price, so this is not a purchase on credit. And al-Allama al-Aqta' said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: It is not permissible for the mudarib to borrow against the mudaraba, and if he does so it is not valid against the owner, do you not see that if he buys with the capital before the sale, the mudarib has recourse on him for the like if it was such, and the owner did not consent to guarantee except the amount of the capital. If we allowed borrowing, it would necessitate him guaranteeing what he did not consent to, and that is not valid. And if his borrowing is not valid against the owner, it is confined to his own liability specifically, and they said: the mudarib may not take a suftaja because that is borrowing, and he does not have the right to borrow, and likewise he may not give a suftaja because that is lending, and he does not have the right to lend, even if he said: act on your opinion. End of the necessary portion.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the limits of a mudarib's authority in a mudaraba (profit-sharing partnership). It opens by stating the general rule: the mudarib cannot lend (iqrad) from the mudaraba capital nor borrow (istidana) on its credit, even if the capital owner says 'act on your opinion' (i'mal bi-ra'ika). The only exception is if the owner explicitly stipulates permission for lending and borrowing. The commentary explains the rationale: lending is a gratuitous act (tabarru') not necessary for trade, like gift and charity, so it falls outside the scope of a general mudaraba agreement. Borrowing would encumber the owner's liability (shughl al-dhimma) beyond the agreed capital, which he did not consent to. The text then details a scenario: if the mudarib borrows after permission, the purchased items are shared equally as a partnership of credit (shirkat wujuh), not mudaraba. It further illustrates the form of borrowing: buying goods on credit after the capital has been used, distinguishing it from cases where the currency type differs but still constitutes a single genus (jins wahid) in monetary value, so it is not considered debt. The passage cites al-Aqta' to emphasize that unauthorized borrowing does not bind the owner; the mudarib is personally liable. It also notes that taking or giving a suftaja (letter of credit) is equivalent to borrowing or lending and is thus prohibited without explicit authorization. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that such acts are impermissible unless specifically allowed by the owner.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudārib): The agent in a mudaraba profit-sharing partnership who manages the capital.
إقراض (iqrāḍ): Lending money from the mudaraba capital to a third party; considered a gratuitous act not part of trade.
استدانة (istidāna): Borrowing money on credit of the mudaraba; encumbers the owner's liability beyond capital.
سفتجة (sufṭaja): A letter of credit or bill of exchange; taking or giving it is equivalent to borrowing or lending.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a mudarib lend money from the mudaraba capital without explicit authorization from the owner?
A) Yes, because mudaraba is a profit-sharing partnership.
B) No, because lending is a gratuitous act not necessary for trade.
C) Yes, if the owner says 'act on your opinion'.
D) No, but only if the capital is in a different currency.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's authority is limited to trade; lending and borrowing require explicit owner authorization.

Mudarib Cannot Recover Transport Costs Using Own Money
Ruling on mudarib's voluntary transport expense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a mudarib who voluntarily spends his own money on transport cannot recover those costs, and distinguish between authorized acts and volunteer acts in mudaraba.

Core Idea
A mudarib's personal expense is voluntary; he cannot charge it to the capital owner even if told 'act on your opinion'.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in mudaraba: the mudarib uses his own funds to transport goods purchased with the mudaraba capital. The text clarifies that such an act is voluntary (mutatawwi'), and the mudarib has no right to reimbursement. This builds on the general prohibition of the mudarib borrowing without explicit permission. Understanding this ruling prevents common misconceptions about the extent of the mudarib's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The mudarib buys cloth (bazz) with mudaraba capital, then washes and transports it to another location using his own money, not the mudaraba capital.
The transport is a post-purchase expense.
2
Apply the rule on borrowing
Using his own money for transport is equivalent to borrowing (istidana) from himself or the owner? The text treats it as borrowing against the owner, which is prohibited without explicit permission.
The mudarib cannot unilaterally impose expenses on the owner.
3
Recognize the volunteer status
The mudarib is declared a volunteer (mutatawwi'); he bears the cost personally and cannot seek reimbursement, even if the owner said 'act on your opinion'.
'Act on your opinion' does not cover lending; it covers trade decisions.
4
Understand the distinction between mudaraba and lending
Mudaraba is a profit-seeking venture, so 'act on your opinion' includes trade expenses, but lending (giving money without return) is not part of it.
The owner's general permission does not authorize the mudarib to lend or to self-finance expenses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المضاربة تعريفها وأحكامها. فلو شرى بالمال بَزَّاً وقَصَر، أو حمل بماله، وقيل له ذلك فقد تطوع وإنما يصح المضاربة باعمل برأيك دون الإقراض؛ لأن المضاربة من صنيع التجار، وهي مجلبة للربح بخلاف الإقراض إذ لا فائدة فيه. قوله: فلو شرى بالمال... الخ؛ هذا تفريع على عدم جواز استدانة المضارب، وتوضيح المسألة: إن المضارب إذا اشترى بمال المضارب ثوب كتان، وغسله وحمله إلى موضع آخر بمال نفسه لا بمال المضاربة، فالمضارب متبرع. وإن قال له المالك: أعمل برأيك، فلا يرجع بماله على رب المال؛ لأنه استدانة على رب المال، والمضارب لا يملكها بقوله: اعمل برأيك بل بالإذن الصريح، وهو لم يوجد. قوله: بزاً؛ بالفتح، قيل: نوع من الثياب، وقيل: الثياب خاصة من أمتعة البيت، وقيل: أمتعة التاجر من الثياب. كذا في المصباح، وقال في الصراح: بز سلاح ومتاع بزاز جامه ومتاع فروش. انتهى. قوله: وقصر: مخففاً ومشدداً، قال في المصباح: قصرت الثوب قصراً. Book of Mudaraba: Its Definition and Rulings. If he buys with the capital cloth (bazz) and then transports (qasr) it, or carries it with his own money, and he is told that, then he has volunteered, and the mudaraba is valid only with 'act on your opinion' not with lending; because mudaraba is from the practice of merchants and it brings profit, unlike lending as there is no benefit in it. His statement: 'If he buys with the capital...' etc.; this is a branch on the impermissibility of the mudarib borrowing, and an explanation of the case: The mudarib, if he buys with the mudarib's capital a linen garment, and washes it and carries it to another place with his own money not with the mudaraba capital, then the mudarib is a volunteer. And if the owner says to him: act on your opinion, he does not recover his money from the owner; because it is borrowing against the owner, and the mudarib does not have the authority for it with the statement 'act on your opinion' but only with explicit permission, which is absent. [1] His statement: 'And the mudaraba only becomes valid...' etc.; this is the difference between mudaraba and lending, where the mudarib has the authority for it when told 'act on your opinion' but not for lending. The summary: mudaraba is from the practice of merchants and it brings profit, so his statement 'act on your opinion' includes it, unlike lending, for it does not bring profit so that statement does not include it.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous lesson by presenting a specific sub-case (tafri') of the general rule that a mudarib cannot borrow. The scenario: the mudarib uses the mudaraba capital to buy cloth (bazz) and then, after washing and processing, transports the goods to another location using his own money (not the mudaraba capital). The ruling is that the mudarib is considered a volunteer (mutatawwi') and cannot recover his transport expenses from the capital owner, even if the owner had told him 'act on your opinion' (i'mal bi-ra'ika). The reasoning is that using his own money for transport amounts to an unauthorized borrowing (istidana) against the owner, which is not permitted by the general instruction. The owner's explicit permission (idhn sarih) would be needed. The passage also provides lexical definitions of 'bazz' (a type of cloth) and 'qasr' (the act of transporting or shortening), clarifying the terminology. Additionally, a final note contrasts mudaraba with lending, emphasizing that mudaraba is a profitable trade activity, hence encompassed by 'act on your opinion', whereas lending yields no profit and is not included.

Key Terms
بز (bazz): A type of cloth or garment, specifically fine textiles or merchant goods.
قصر (qasr): To transport or carry; also to shorten (as in shortening a garment). In context, it means transporting the goods after purchase.
متبرع (mutatawwiʿ): Volunteer; one who performs an act without right to compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No, because it is considered a voluntary act without right to reimbursement.
D) No, unless the mudarib obtains a written agreement.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib cannot recover personal expenses incurred voluntarily; general permission does not authorize lending or self-funding.

Dyeing Black in Mudarabah: Custom and the Scope of 'Work with Your Judgement'
Ruling on mudarib dyeing cloth with own money under general permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mufta bihi ruling on dyeing black in mudarabah differs from Abu Hanifa's original view and apply the role of custom ('urf) in determining trade practices.

Core Idea
Custom changes the ruling: black dye was a defect in Abu Hanifa's time but not today.

The owner's permission 'work with your judgement' seems broad, but classical Hanafi jurists debated its limits. This passage examines whether dyeing cloth black is covered, revealing how custom ('urf) shapes fiqh rulings. Understanding this dispute will help you see how Hanafi fiqh adapts without abandoning its principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on red dye
Identify the ruling when the mudarib dyes the cloth red: it falls under 'work with your judgement', he becomes a partner in the increase, and is not liable.
This is the agreed-upon base ruling from the zahir al-riwayah.
2
Identify the dispute on black dye
Recognize that Abu Hanifa holds black is excluded because it is a deficiency, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) include it, treating it like red.
The dispute is recorded in the text: 'لا يدخل تحت اعمل برأيك عند أبي حنيفة'.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument: black dye can be a trade practice that adds value depending on custom.
The text implies their reasoning is based on the potential for value increase, similar to other colors.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and resolution
Note how the text reconciles the dispute: it is a difference of time (ikhtilaf zaman). In Abu Hanifa's era, black was considered a deficiency; in later times, it is not.
The key phrase: 'التحقيق: أن هذا اختلاف زمان وفي زماننا: لا يعد نقصاً'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position today is that all colors, including black, are covered by 'work with your judgement'. The mudarib becomes a partner in the increase.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): according to Abu Hanifa, the mudarib is a volunteer (mutatawwi); according to the Sahibayn, he is a partner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن صبغه أحمر فهو شريك بما زاد ودخل تحت اعمل برأيك كالخلطة، فلا يضمن المضارب. وإنما قال: فصبغه أحمر، حتى لو صبغه أسود، فإنه لا يدخل تحت اعمل برأيك عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن السواد نقصان عنده، وأما سائر الألوان غير السواد فكالحمرة، فلا يضمن المضارب. قوله: فلا يضمن... الخ؛ يعني فلا يضمن المضارب بصبغ ذلك الثوب أحمر. والتحقيق: أن هذا اختلاف زمان وفي زماننا: لا يعد نقصاً فهو كالحمرة، فيدخل في: اعمل برأيك سائر الألوان كالحمرة. كما صرحوا به، وقد تقدم ذكره في «كتاب الغصب» فتذكر.
And if he dyes it red, he is a partner in the increase, and it falls under 'work with your judgement' like mixing, so the mudarib is not liable. And he only said 'he dyes it red' because if he dyes it black, it does not fall under 'work with your judgement' according to Abu Hanifa, because black is a deficiency according to him. As for colors other than black, they are like red, so the mudarib is not liable. His statement: 'so he is not liable' etc. means the mudarib is not liable for dyeing that cloth red. And the verification: this is a difference of time, and in our time, black is not considered a deficiency, so it is like red, and it falls under 'work with your judgement' all colors like red. As they have explicitly stated, and it was previously mentioned in the Book of Ghasb, so remember.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage addresses the scope of the mudarib's authority when the owner of capital says 'work with your judgement'. The ruling states that if the mudarib buys cloth and dyes it red with his own money, he becomes a partner in the increase in value caused by the dye. He is not a volunteer (mutatawwi) because dyeing red is a trade practice that enhances value, and the owner's general permission covers it. However, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, dyeing it black does not fall under this permission because black is considered a deficiency in value in his time. Therefore, if the mudarib dyes black, he does so at his own risk and is not a partner. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed; they considered black to be like other colors in terms of potential increase. The text notes that this is a difference based on custom ('urf) that changes over time. In later times, black is no longer viewed as a deficiency, so the ruling follows the opinion of the two companions. This principle—that commercial customs evolve—is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh and allows rulings to adapt while preserving the underlying legal framework.

Key Terms
اعمل برأيك (i'mal bi-ra'yik): A phrase from the owner allowing the mudarib to use his own judgement in trade decisions.
صبغ (sabgh): Dyeing cloth; in this context, adding dye from the mudarib's own money to the mudarabah asset.
نقصان (nuqsan): A decrease in value; in this ruling, black dye was considered a defect that lowers the cloth's market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mudarib is a volunteer and cannot recover his costs.
B) The mudarib becomes a partner in the increase, because black is no longer considered a deficiency.
C) The mudarib is liable for the entire cloth.
D) The mudarib must seek explicit permission from the owner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on black dye in mudarabah changed from excluded to included because custom ('urf) evolved, illustrating the school's principle of legal adaptation.

Dyeing and Mixing in Mudarabah: Partnership in Increase
Mudarabah rulings on dyeing and mixing under general authorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mudarib dyes cloth with his own money under general authorization, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding black dye.

Core Idea
Dyeing cloth red under 'اعمل برأيك' makes the mudarib a partner in the increase; black dye is now included.

In a mudarabah contract, the capital owner may authorize the mudarib broadly with the phrase 'اعمل برأيك' (work as you see fit). What actions does this permission cover? This lesson examines the ruling when the mudarib uses his own funds to dye a cloth purchased from the mudarabah capital, and how the proceeds are split. It also addresses the historical dispute over black dye and the distinction between mixing property and providing services like laundering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and authorization
The mudarib dyes a cloth with his own money after being told 'اعمل برأيك'.
This is a permissible act within the scope of agency.
2
Determine the ruling
He becomes a partner in the increase from the dye; he is not liable for loss.
The increase is the added value, not just the cost.
3
Distinguish from laundering
Laundering does not create partnership because no property is mixed.
The key is mixing property (خلطة) vs. providing a service.
4
Analyze the black dye dispute
Abu Hanifa excludes black; the Sahibayn include it; the later view includes all colors due to custom change.
The dispute is resolved by noting the change in custom over time.
5
Apply the distribution rule
When sold, the mudarib gets the value of his dye, the mudarabah capital gets the base value of the cloth.
Example: 1000 dirhams base, 1500 dyed → 1000 to capital, 500 to mudarib.

Source Text
