[1] ( وإن صبغه أحمر فهو شريك بما زادَ ، ودخل تحت اعمل برأيك كالخلطة ) : أي إذا قال : اعمل برأيك فصبغَهُ أحمر يكون شريكاً بما زاد ويدخلُ الصبغ تحت اعمل برأيك، وكذا الخلطة بماله بخلاف القصارة ؛ لأنه لا يختلط به شيء من ماله ، وإنما قال: فصبغه ،أحمر، حتى لو صبغه أسود، فإنه لا يدخل تحت اعمل برأيك عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنَّ السَّوادِ نقصان عنده، وأما سائر الألوان غير السواد فكالحمرة، فلا يضمن المضارب ) : أي بصبغه أحمر، وبالخلط بماله إذا قال : اعمل برأيك
[E] بيضته والقصارة الصناعة الفاعل : قصار. انتهى. وقال في «الصراح» في تحقيق لفظ القصر : جامه کاذری کردن ومنه القصار كاذر. انتهى. وفي تحقيق لفظ التقصير: تقصير الثوب : كوفتن جامه. انتهى.
[11 ] قوله : وإن صبغه ... الخ ؛ يعني وإن اشترى ثوباً فصبغه أحمر من ماله لا يكون متبرعاً ، بل يكون شريكاً بما زاد الصبغ فيه، ويدخل تحت قوله : اعمل برأيك، فيقسمُ بعد البيع ثمنه على قيمة صبغ المضارب، وقيمة الثوب الأبيض باختلاف القصارة والحمل، فإنّه لا يصير شريكاً بهما، إذ ليسا بمال قائم ، حتى لو قصر بالنشا صـار شريكاً. كما صرحوا به.
[٢]قوله : كالخلطة ؛ يعني كما جاز للمضارب الخلط بماله إذا قال له ربُّ المال : اعمل برأيك.
(۳) قوله : وإنّما قال.... الخ ؛ أي إنّما قيد قوله : صبغه بقوله : أحمر ؛ لأنه لو صبغه أسود لا يدخل تحت قوله : اعمل برأيك عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لكون السواد نقصاناً عنده خلافاً لصاحبيه. والتحقيق: أن هذا اختلاف زمان وفي زماننا : لا يعد نقصاً فهو كالحمرة، فيدخل في : اعمل برأيك ، سائر الألوان كالحمرة. كما صرحوا به، وقد تقدم ذكره في «كتاب الغصب» فتذكر.
[٤]قوله : فلا يضمن... الخ ؛ يعني فلا يضمن المضارب بصبغ ذلك الثوب أحمر
كتاب المضاربة تعريفها وأحكامها
وله حصة صبغِهِ إن بيع، وحصّةُ الثَّوبِ في المضاربة) : أي في مال المضاربة.
(ولا أن يجاوز" بلداً أو سلعةً أو وقتاً أو شخصاً عينه رب المال وخلط رأس المال بماله إذا قال له المالك : اعمل برأيك، فإن هذا القول يتضمنه، فلا يكون به متعدّياً.
[ 1 ] قوله : وله ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ المضارب يصير شريكاً لرب المال بما زاد الصبغ فيه ، وحصة قيمة الصبغ يكون له إذا بيع الثوب المصبوغ ، وحصة الثوب الأبيض في مال المضاربة، حتى إذا كانت قيمة الثوب غير مصبوغ ألف درهم، ومصبوغاً ألف وخمسمئة كانت الألف للمضاربة، وخمسمئة للمضارب بدل ماله وهو الصبغ.
[٢] قوله : ولا أن يجاوز... الخ ؛ عطف على قوله : ولا أن يقرض ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يجاوز عمّا عيّنه رب المال من بلد أو سلعة أو وقت أو شخص ؛ فإنّ عقد المضاربة توكيل، وفي التخصيص فائدة ؛ لكون التجارات مختلفة باختلاف الأمكنة والأوقات والأمتعة والأشخاص. بخلاف ما إذا قيده بسوق معين من المصر، حيث لا يتقيد به ؛ فإنَّ المصر الواحد قل ما يتفاوت جوانبه وأسواقه ؛ لكونه كبقعةٍ واحدة، فلا يفيد التقييد إلا إذا صرّح بالنهي بأن قال له : اعمل في هذا السوق، ولا تعمل في غيره ، فحينئذ يتقيد به ؛ لأنَّ رب المال له ولاية التصرُّف إليه. بخلاف ما إذا قال : بع نسيئة ولا تبع حالاً ، حيث كان له أن يبيعه حالاً عند عدم اختلاف السعر بينهما ؛ لأنه مخالفة إلى خير بيقين ، وهذا كما إذا وكل شخصاً ، بألف درهم، ونهى عن البيع بالزيادة، فباعه الوكيلُ بألفين جاز ؛ لكونه مخالفة إلى خير، وقد مرَّ بحثه في «كتاب الوكالة، فظهر لك أنَّ التقييد المفيد يعتبر، وغيره لا. ثم اعلم أنَّ ما يفيد التقييد من الألفاظ ستة :
الأوّل: خذه مضاربة تعمل به في مصر، فقوله: تعمل ، تفسير لقوله : خذه مضاربة، والكلام المبهم إذا تعقبه تفسير كان الحكم للتفسير.
والثاني : خذه مضاربة فاعمل به في مصر فهو أيضاً في معنى التفسير؛ لكون الفاء للوصل والتعقب ، وما تعقب الكلام المبهم يكون تفسيراً له.
And if he dyes it red, he is a partner in the increase, and it falls under 'work as you see fit' like mixing, so the mudarib is not liable. [1] (And if he dyes it red, he is a partner in the increase, and it falls under 'work as you see fit' like mixing): meaning if he says: work as you see fit, and he dyes it red, he becomes a partner in the increase and the dye enters under 'work as you see fit'. Similarly, mixing with his own property, unlike laundering because nothing of his property mixes with it. He only said: so he dyes it red, so that if he dyes it black, it does not enter under 'work as you see fit' according to Abu Hanifa because black is a deficiency in his view. As for other colors besides black, they are like red. So the mudarib is not liable: i.e., for dyeing it red and mixing with his own property if he says: work as you see fit. [E] Its whitening and laundering are a craft, the doer is a launderer. End. And al-Sirah said in verifying the word 'qasr': to full cloth, hence the launderer. End. And in verifying the word 'taqsir': taqsir of the garment: to beat cloth. End. [11] His saying: And if he dyes etc.: meaning if he buys a cloth and dyes it red from his own money, he is not a volunteer, but rather he is a partner in the increase of the dye, and it enters under his saying: work as you see fit. So after sale, its price is divided according to the value of the mudarib's dye and the value of the white cloth, differing by laundering and carrying, for he does not become a partner in these since they are not a lasting property, unless he pre-shrinks with starch, then he becomes a partner, as they have clearly stated. [2] His saying: like mixing: meaning just as it is permissible for the mudarib to mix with his property if the capital owner says to him: work as you see fit. (3) His saying: He only said etc.: meaning he restricted his saying 'he dyes it' by his saying 'red' because if he dyes it black, it does not enter under his saying: work as you see fit according to Abu Hanifa, because black is a deficiency in his view, contrary to his two companions. The verification is that this is a difference of time, and in our time it is not considered a deficiency, so it is like red, so it enters under 'work as you see fit', all other colors like red, as they have clearly stated. It has previously been mentioned in the Book of Ghasp, so remember. [4] His saying: So he is not liable etc.: meaning the mudarib is not liable for dyeing that cloth red. The book of Mudarabah: its definition and rulings. And he has the share of his dye if sold, and the share of the cloth in the mudarabah: i.e., in the mudarabah capital. And (he may not exceed) a city, or commodity, or time, or person specified by the capital owner, and mixing the capital with his own property if the owner says to him: work as you see fit, for this saying includes it, so he is not thereby transgressing. [1] His saying: And he has etc.: meaning the mudarib becomes a partner with the capital owner in the increase of the dye, and the share of the value of the dye belongs to him when the dyed cloth is sold, and the share of the white cloth is in the mudarabah capital, such that if the value of the cloth undyed is a thousand dirhams, and dyed it is a thousand five hundred, then the thousand is for the mudarabah and the five hundred is for the mudarib as compensation for his property, which is the dye. [2] His saying: And he may not exceed etc.: it is conjunct to his saying: and he may not lend; meaning it is not for the mudarib to exceed what the capital owner specified of city, commodity, time, or person; for the contract of mudarabah is an agency, and in specification there is benefit because trades differ by difference of places, times, goods, and persons. Unlike if he restricts him to a specific market of the city, where he is not bound by it; for the single city rarely differs in its sides and markets, being like a single locality, so the restriction does not benefit unless he explicitly forbids by saying: work in this market and do not work in another, then he is bound by it because the capital owner has authority over his disposition. Unlike if he says: sell on credit and do not sell for cash, where he may sell for cash when there is no difference in price between them, because it is a deviation to certain good. This is like if he appoints an agent for a thousand dirhams and prohibits selling at an increase, then the agent sells for two thousand, it is valid because it is a deviation to good. Its discussion has passed in the Book of Agency. So it is clear to you that beneficial restriction is considered, and other is not. Then know that the expressions that effect a beneficial restriction are six: First: 'Take it as mudarabah, work with it in Egypt', so his saying 'work' is an explanation of his saying 'take it as mudarabah', and ambiguous speech when followed by an explanation, the ruling is according to the explanation. Second: 'Take it as mudarabah and work with it in Egypt' is also in the sense of explanation because the 'fa' is for conjunction and sequence, and what follows ambiguous speech is an explanation for it.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings on mudarabah from the commentary 'Umda al-Ri'aya'. It covers three main areas: (1) Dyeing cloth by the mudarib with his own money, (2) Distribution of proceeds when the dyed cloth is sold, and (3) Restrictions on the mudarib's authority to exceed specified limits set by the capital owner. 

The first section (from the beginning through the black dye discussion) establishes that if the capital owner authorizes the mudarib with 'work as you see fit' (اعمل برأيك), the mudarib may dye a purchased cloth red with his own money, and he becomes a partner in the increase (the added value from dyeing). This is similar to mixing his own property with the mudarabah capital. However, laundering (القصارة) and carrying costs are not included because they are not tangible property. The madhhab dispute regarding black dye: Abu Hanifa holds that black dye is not covered by 'اعمل برأيك' because it is considered a deficiency; the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. The commentator notes that this is a difference of time—in their era, black was a deficiency, but in the commentator's time it is not, so it is included. 
The second section (وله حصة صبغه إن بيع) specifies the distribution: when the dyed cloth is sold, the mudarib receives the share of the dye's value, and the cloth's value (as white) goes to the mudarabah capital. An example is given: if undyed cloth is worth 1000 dirhams and dyed it is worth 1500, the 1000 is mudarabah capital and the 500 is the mudarib's compensation for his dye. 
The third section (ولا أن يجاوز...) states that the mudarib cannot exceed the capital owner's specification of a city, commodity, time, or person, because mudarabah is a type of agency and specification has benefit. However, restrictions that offer no real benefit (like specifying a particular market within a city) are not binding unless explicitly stated with a prohibition. The mudarib may deviate from a restriction if it leads to certain benefit (e.g., selling for cash when credit is prohibited but cash price is the same, or selling at a higher price when prohibited to increase). The passage concludes by listing six expressions that constitute beneficial restrictions, illustrating how language can create binding limitations.

Key Terms
اعمل برأيك (i‘mal bi-ra’yik): Authorization phrase meaning 'work as you see fit'; grants the mudarib broad discretion within trade norms.
الخلطة (al-khulta): Mixing the mudarabah capital with the mudarib's own property; permissible if authorized, making the mudarib a partner in profit.
القصارة (al-qaṣāra): Laundering or fulling of cloth; a service that does not make the mudarib a partner as no property mixes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant Hanafi view today, is black dye included under 'اعمل برأيك'?
A) No, because it is a deficiency
B) Yes, because custom has changed and it is not a deficiency
C) Only if the capital owner explicitly allows it
D) It depends on the contract

Key Takeaway
Under general authorization, the mudarib becomes a partner in any increase from using his own property to improve the capital, but not from mere services.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
7 min
Beneficial Restrictions on the Mudarib: When Can the Capital Owner Limit Authority?
Mudarabah rulings on beneficial restrictions and when they bind the mudarib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which restrictions on the mudarib are binding and why, and apply the principle of beneficial restriction.

Core Idea
Only restrictions that provide a tangible benefit in trade bind the mudarib; deviations to certain good are permitted.

The capital owner may specify a city, commodity, time, or person for the mudarib's trading activities. But are all such specifications binding? This lesson explores the Hanafi principle of 'beneficial restriction' (التقييد المفيد) and when the mudarib may deviate for certain benefit. The passage also lists the six expressions that create binding restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the restriction types
The capital owner may restrict city, commodity, time, or person.
These are the typical categories.
2
Apply the principle of beneficial restriction
Only restrictions that offer a real benefit in trade are binding.
Trivial restrictions (e.g., a specific market within a city) are not binding unless explicitly prohibited.
3
Distinguish between binding and non-binding restrictions
A restriction binds if it provides a tangible advantage; if not, it is non-binding unless the owner says 'I forbid anything else'.
Example: specifying a city is beneficial, but a market within a city is not.
4
Analyze exceptions for deviation to certain good
If deviation yields certain benefit (e.g., selling for cash when credit was specified, but price same), it is permitted.
This is like an agent exceeding authority for a clear advantage.

[1] His saying: And he has etc.: meaning the mudarib becomes a partner with the capital owner in the increase of the dye, and the share of the value of the dye belongs to him when the dyed cloth is sold, and the share of the white cloth is in the mudarabah capital, such that if the value of the cloth undyed is a thousand dirhams, and dyed it is a thousand five hundred, then the thousand is for the mudarabah and the five hundred is for the mudarib as compensation for his property, which is the dye. [2] His saying: And he may not exceed etc.: it is conjunct to his saying: and he may not lend; meaning it is not for the mudarib to exceed what the capital owner specified of city, commodity, time, or person; for the contract of mudarabah is an agency, and in specification there is benefit because trades differ by difference of places, times, goods, and persons. Unlike if he restricts him to a specific market of the city, where he is not bound by it; for the single city rarely differs in its sides and markets, being like a single locality, so the restriction does not benefit unless he explicitly forbids by saying: work in this market and do not work in another, then he is bound by it because the capital owner has authority over his disposition. Unlike if he says: sell on credit and do not sell for cash, where he may sell for cash when there is no difference in price between them, because it is a deviation to certain good. This is like if he appoints an agent for a thousand dirhams and prohibits selling at an increase, then the agent sells for two thousand, it is valid because it is a deviation to good. Its discussion has passed in the Book of Agency. So it is clear to you that beneficial restriction is considered, and other is not. Then know that the expressions that effect a beneficial restriction are six: First: 'Take it as mudarabah, work with it in Egypt', so his saying 'work' is an explanation of his saying 'take it as mudarabah', and ambiguous speech when followed by an explanation, the ruling is according to the explanation. Second: 'Take it as mudarabah and work with it in Egypt' is also in the sense of explanation because the 'fa' is for conjunction and sequence, and what follows ambiguous speech is an explanation for it.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings on mudarabah from the commentary 'Umda al-Ri'aya'. It covers three main areas: (1) Dyeing cloth by the mudarib with his own money, (2) Distribution of proceeds when the dyed cloth is sold, and (3) Restrictions on the mudarib's authority to exceed specified limits set by the capital owner. 

The first section (from the beginning through the black dye discussion) establishes that if the capital owner authorizes the mudarib with 'work as you see fit' (اعمل برأيك), the mudarib may dye a purchased cloth red with his own money, and he becomes a partner in the increase (the added value from dyeing). This is similar to mixing his own property with the mudarabah capital. However, laundering (القصارة) and carrying costs are not included because they are not tangible property. The madhhab dispute regarding black dye: Abu Hanifa holds that black dye is not covered by 'اعمل برأيك' because it is considered a deficiency; the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. The commentator notes that this is a difference of time—in their era, black was a deficiency, but in the commentator's time it is not, so it is included. 
The second section (وله حصة صبغه إن بيع) specifies the distribution: when the dyed cloth is sold, the mudarib receives the share of the dye's value, and the cloth's value (as white) goes to the mudarabah capital. An example is given: if undyed cloth is worth 1000 dirhams and dyed it is worth 1500, the 1000 is mudarabah capital and the 500 is the mudarib's compensation for his dye. 
The third section (ولا أن يجاوز...) states that the mudarib cannot exceed the capital owner's specification of a city, commodity, time, or person, because mudarabah is a type of agency and specification has benefit. However, restrictions that offer no real benefit (like specifying a particular market within a city) are not binding unless explicitly stated with a prohibition. The mudarib may deviate from a restriction if it leads to certain benefit (e.g., selling for cash when credit is prohibited but cash price is the same, or selling at a higher price when prohibited to increase). The passage concludes by listing six expressions that constitute beneficial restrictions, illustrating how language can create binding limitations.

Key Terms
التقييد المفيد (al-taqyīd al-mufīd): Beneficial restriction; a condition set by the capital owner that provides a tangible benefit in trade, thus binding the mudarib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a beneficial restriction that binds the mudarib?
A) 'Sell in the main market of the city' without explicit prohibition
B) 'Only sell dates, not any other commodity'
C) 'Work only on Mondays'
D) 'Trade in Syria' without specifying a city

Key Takeaway
A restriction is binding only if it provides a tangible benefit; the mudarib may deviate if it leads to certain good.

Mudarib's Share and Binding Restrictions in Mudarabah
Commentary on mudarib's authority limits and binding wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for the mudarib's share in enhanced capital goods and distinguish between binding and non-binding restrictions using Hanafi linguistic criteria.

Core Idea
The mudarib owns the added value from his own material; only six specific linguistic forms create binding restrictions.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies two critical aspects of mudarabah: first, how the mudarib's own contribution (like dye) gives him a proportional share in the increase; second, the nuanced rules determining which instructions from the capital owner are binding. The commentator provides detailed linguistic criteria for effective restrictions, drawing analogies from agency law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudarib's right in enhanced goods
When the mudarib adds his own material (e.g., dye) to the capital, the increase in value belongs to him. Separate the original capital value from the added value.
Example: cloth worth 1000 undyed, 1500 dyed → 1000 for capital, 500 for mudarib.
2
Classify the types of restrictions
The owner may restrict location, commodity, time, or person. Not all restrictions are binding; some are mere advice.
3
Analyze the six effective expressions
Expressions using tafsir (explanation), faa (succession), laam (purpose), baa (adhesion), fee (locality), or alaa (condition) impose binding conditions.
E.g., 'Take it as mudarabah on condition that you work in Egypt' is binding.
4
Analyze the two ineffective expressions
Expressions using waw (and) or an independent sentence after the contract are advice, not conditions.
E.g., 'Take it as mudarabah and work in Egypt' is non-binding.
5
State the consequence of exceeding binding restrictions
If the mudarib exceeds a binding restriction, he becomes an usurper: the transaction is for his own account, he takes profit but bears loss, and he is liable for the capital.

Second: Take it as mudarabah, so work with it in Egypt; it is also in the sense of explanation because the faa is for connection and succession, and what follows ambiguous speech becomes an explanation of it. Fourth: Take it as mudarabah for half in Egypt, for the baa is for adhesion, so it requires that the work be therein. Fifth: Take it as mudarabah for half in Egypt; for the word fee is for locality, and it is only locality if the doer and the action occur in it. Sixth: Take it as mudarabah on condition that you work in Egypt, for alaa is for condition, so he is bound by it. And what does not benefit it are several expressions: 1. Including his saying: Take it as mudarabah and work with it in Egypt; this restriction does not benefit such that liability is not incumbent on the mudarib for working in other than that city; because the origin of the waw is that it is not except for conjunction, and a thing is not conjoined to itself but to something else, and it may be for beginning if it is after a sentence, so this sentence is of the type of advice not of the type of condition. 2. And the rule in it is that whenever the owner of capital mentions after mudarabah what cannot be uttered initially, and can be made based on what precedes, it is made based on it as in the six expressions; and if it is proper to begin with it, it is not based on what precedes and is made as an independent beginning as in the latter two expressions. So the mudarib then may work in the city he specifies or in another. I have mentioned here what you cannot do without. (1) His saying: If he exceeds... etc.; meaning that if the mudarib exceeds what the owner of capital designated by going out to another country than the one designated by the owner, and transacted therein, buying a commodity other than what was designated, or in a time other than what was designated, or from someone other than whom was designated, the mudarib is liable because he acted without his order, becoming an usurper, and the purchased item is for him, and he has its profit derived from it, and his loss upon him.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of mudarabah, particularly concerning the mudarib's share in the increased value of goods (such as dye) and the limits on his authority. The commentator begins by explaining that if a mudarib dyes a cloth using his own dye, he becomes a partner in the increase: the value of the undyed cloth belongs to the mudarabah capital, and the added value from the dye belongs to the mudarib. This is illustrated with an example of a cloth worth 1000 dirhams undyed and 1500 dyed. 

The main subject is the restriction on the mudarib: he may not exceed the owner's specifications regarding location, commodity, time, or person. The commentary draws from the principle that mudarabah is a type of agency (wakalah), and specification has benefit because profits vary. However, not every restriction is binding. For instance, restricting to a particular market in a city is not binding unless explicitly forbidding other markets, because the city is considered one area. Similarly, if the owner says 'sell on credit, not for cash,' the mudarib may still sell for cash if prices are equal, as this is a beneficial deviation, analogous to an agent selling for a higher price despite being forbidden. 

Then, the commentator provides a detailed linguistic analysis of six expressions that create effective restrictions and two that do not. The effective ones: (1) 'Take it as mudarabah to work with it in Egypt' (using the verb 'to work' as an explanation); (2) 'Take it as mudarabah, so work with it in Egypt' (using 'faa' for succession); (3) 'Take it as mudarabah to work with it in Egypt' (using 'laam' for purpose); (4) 'Take it as mudarabah for half in Egypt' (using 'baa' for adhesion); (5) 'Take it as mudarabah for half in Egypt' (using 'fee' for locality); (6) 'Take it as mudarabah on condition that you work in Egypt' (using 'alaa' for condition). The ineffective ones: (1) 'Take it as mudarabah and work with it in Egypt' (using 'waw' for conjunction, making it advice); (2) 'Work in Kufa' after the contract (separate sentence, likely advice). The general rule: if the owner says something after the contract that cannot stand alone as an independent command, it is taken as a binding condition; if it can stand alone, it is advice. 

Finally, the consequence of exceeding: the mudarib becomes liable for the capital (as an usurper), but the profit from the transaction belongs to him, and he bears any loss.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The partner who manages the capital in a mudarabah contract.
صبغ (sibgh): Dye or any material added by the mudarib that increases the value of the capital goods.
التقييد المفيد (al-taqyid al-mufid): A restriction in the mudarabah contract that is beneficial and binding, expressed in specific linguistic forms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mudarib must work only in Egypt; if he works elsewhere, he is liable.
B) The mudarib may work in Egypt or elsewhere without liability.
C) The mudarib must work in Egypt or pay compensation.
D) The mudarib cannot work anywhere without additional permission.

Key Takeaway
In mudarabah, only restrictions expressed in the six specific linguistic forms are binding; the mudarib is not liable for disregarding advice or beneficial deviations.

Six Wordings for Mudarabah Restrictions: Binding vs Advisory
Classification of wordings for mudarabah location restrictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle to classify any wording used by the capital owner in a mudarabah contract as either a binding condition or mere advice.

Core Idea
Only wordings that cannot stand alone as independent sentences bind the mudarib.

When a capital owner specifies a location in a mudarabah contract, the legal effect depends on the wording's grammatical structure. The Hanafi school has a clear principle: only wordings that cannot be uttered independently at the start of a sentence are appended as conditions; those that can stand alone are treated as separate advice. Understanding this classification is crucial for determining the mudarib's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the capital owner's wording
Look at the exact phrase used after the mudarabah contract. Is it a prepositional phrase like 'to work in Egypt', 'with half in Egypt', 'in Egypt', or 'on condition that you work in Egypt'? Or is it a sentence like 'and work in Egypt' or an independent imperative 'Work in Kufa'?
The wording determines whether it is appended or independent.
2
Check independent utterance test
Can the wording be uttered as a complete sentence at the beginning of a conversation? If yes (e.g., 'Work in Kufa'), it is an independent statement of advice. If no (e.g., 'to work in Egypt' – cannot start a sentence with 'to'), it is a condition appended to the mudarabah.
If the wording includes a conjunction like waw that cannot conjoin the same thing, it becomes a new sentence.
3
Classify the wording: binding or advisory
If the wording is dependent, classify it as binding. The binding prepositions are lam (purpose), ba' (adhesion), fi (container), and 'ala (condition). If independent, it is advisory – the mudarib may ignore it without liability.
The passage lists six examples: (3)-(6) are binding; (1)-(2) are advisory.
4
Determine the legal consequence
For binding: the mudarib must comply; if he works elsewhere, he becomes liable (daman) for the capital and keeps his profit. For advisory: the mudarib may choose any location without liability.
The ruling for exceeding a binding condition is "he is liable and his profit is his".

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he exceeds it, he is liable and his profit is his. The third: Take it as a mudarabah to work with it in Egypt; this is apparent. The fourth: Take it as a mudarabah with half in Egypt, so the ba' [preposition] indicates adhesion, necessitating that the work be in it. The fifth: Take it as a mudarabah with half in Egypt, for the word 'fi' indicates container, and it is only a container if the subject and verb occur in it. The sixth: Take it as a mudarabah on condition that you work in Egypt, for 'ala' indicates condition, so he is bound by it. And what does not bind it: several wordings: 1. Among them is his saying: Take it as a mudarabah and work with it in Egypt; this restriction has no effect, so the mudarib is not liable for working in a different city, because the original rule for waw is that it is only for conjunction, and a thing is not conjoined to itself but to another, and it may be for commencement if after a sentence, so this sentence is of the category of consultation, not condition. 2. Among them is his saying: Work in Kufa, after his saying: Take it as a mudarabah; this is also interpreted as advice, not condition. The principle is that whenever the capital owner mentions after the mudarabah something that cannot be uttered initially and can be made dependent on what precedes, it is made dependent, as in the six wordings. If it can stand alone, it is not made dependent and is considered a new sentence, as in the last two wordings. Then the mudarib may work in the designated city or another. And I have mentioned here what you need; if you want more detail, refer to the commentaries of al-Kanz and the glosses of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya explains the legal effect of different wordings used by the capital owner (rabb al-mal) when specifying the location or conditions of a mudarabah contract. It begins by stating the general ruling: if the mudarib exceeds the specified location, he is liable for the capital and keeps his profit (the profit is his, but he bears losses). Then it lists three wordings that create binding conditions: (3) 'Take it as a mudarabah to work with it in Egypt' – this is clear because the lam of purpose indicates the work must be in Egypt. (4) 'Take it as a mudarabah with half in Egypt' – the ba' indicates adhesion, meaning the work itself is tied to Egypt. (5) 'Take it as a mudarabah with half in Egypt' – fi indicates container, so Egypt is the container for the work. (6) 'Take it as a mudarabah on condition that you work in Egypt' – 'ala explicitly indicates a condition. Then it mentions two wordings that do not create a binding condition: (1) 'Take it as a mudarabah and work with it in Egypt' – the waw (and) is for conjunction, and since it cannot conjoin the same thing, it is interpreted as a separate sentence of advice, not a condition. (2) 'Work in Kufa' after the mudarabah contract – this is also mere advice. The principle is: if the capital owner says something after the mudarabah that cannot be said independently at the start (like specifying a city without the contract wording), it is appended to the contract and becomes a condition. But if it can be said independently, it remains a separate statement of advice. Thus the mudarib is free to work elsewhere. The passage ends by directing the reader to other works for more detail.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits according to an agreed ratio, with losses borne by the capital owner.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the mudarib becomes liable for the capital if he acts beyond the authority given, making him a guarantor of the capital.
ظرف (zarf): Container; in grammatical sense, the location where the action takes place, indicating that the work must occur in that place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mudarabah contract, if the capital owner says 'Take it as a mudarabah and work with it in Egypt', what is the legal effect?
A) The mudarib must work only in Egypt, otherwise he is liable.
B) The mudarib may work anywhere because the wording is advice, not a condition.
C) The mudarib must return the capital immediately.
D) The mudarib shares profit equally with the capital owner.

Key Takeaway
Only wordings that cannot stand alone as independent sentences create binding conditions in mudarabah; those that can are mere advice.

Liability for Exceeding Specified Location
Commentary on liability when mudarib exceeds conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a mudarib exceeds specified conditions, explain the rationale using the analogy to ghasb, and apply the exception if the capital is returned before transacting.

Core Idea
Violating a capital owner’s condition makes the mudarib a usurper, liable for capital but owner of unauthorized profit.

In mudarabah, the mudarib is a trustee (amin) over the capital, but his authority is limited by the contract. If he steps outside those limits—like going to a different city or buying a different commodity—he loses his trustee status. This lesson explores the legal consequences: he becomes a usurper (ghasib), liable for losses, yet any profit from unauthorized dealings is his. However, if he returns to compliance before transacting, his trust is restored.

Guided Path
1
Identify the violating act
Determine which condition the mudarib exceeded: specified city, commodity, time, or person. The violation must be of a condition set by the capital owner at the time of the contract.
If no condition was specified, the mudarib has broader discretion and is not liable for mere choice of location.
2
Apply the ruling of liability
The mudarib becomes liable (damin) for the capital because he acted without permission. He is now treated as a usurper (ghasib). The capital owner can demand its return regardless of profit or loss.
Liability is strict: even if the unauthorized transaction yields profit, the mudarib must still return the capital.
3
Determine fate of unauthorized purchase
Any goods bought in the unauthorized transaction belong to the mudarib personally. Likewise, any profit from that transaction is his, and any loss is his alone. The capital owner has no claim to that profit.
This is because the mudarib acted outside the agency, so the transaction is for his own account.
4
Apply the exception
If the mudarib does not engage in any transaction and returns the capital to the specified city (or otherwise rectifies), his liability is lifted. He becomes a trustee again, and the mudarabah continues as before.
The exception requires that no unauthorized act has occurred—mere intention to exceed is not enough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فإن جاوز ... الخ ؛ يعني فإن تجاوز المضارب عما عينه ربُّ المال بأن خرج المضارب إلى غير ذلك البلد الذي عينه ربُّ المال فتصرف فيه واشترى سلعة غير ما عينه، أو في وقت غير ما عينه، أو باع من غير من عينه، ضمن المضارب ؛ لأنه تصرف بغير أمره ، فصار غاصباً، وكان المشترى له وله ربحه الذي حصل منه، وعليه خسرانه. وإن لم يتصرف فيه حتى ردَّه إلى البلد الذي عينه برئ من الضمان ؛ لأنه أمين خالف ثم عاد إلى الوفاق، ورجع المالُ مضاربة على حاله ؛ لأن المال باق في يده بالعقد السابق، وهذا إذا صدر من رب المال عند عقد المضاربة.
His statement: 'If he exceeds it, etc.' means: If the mudarib exceeds what the capital owner specified, e.g., by going to a different city than the one specified, and transacts there, buying a different commodity, or at a different time, or from a different person, the mudarib is liable because he acted without permission, thus becoming a usurper. The purchased items are his, and he gets the profit from it, and he bears the loss. If he does not transact with it until he returns it to the specified city, he is released from liability because he was a trustee who violated then returned to compliance, and the capital returns to mudarabah as before because the capital remains in his hand under the prior contract. This is if the specification was made by the capital owner at the time of the mudarabah contract.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage comments on the earlier ruling about exceeding specified conditions in mudarabah. The commentator explains that if the mudarib violates the capital owner's restriction—by going to a different city, buying a different commodity, at a different time, or from a different person—he becomes liable for the capital. The reasoning is that he acted without authorization, making him a usurper (ghasib). Therefore, any item purchased in this violation belongs to him personally, and he is entitled to any profit from it, but also bears any loss. However, if the mudarib does not actually transact in the unauthorized manner and returns the capital to the specified city, his liability is removed. This is because he was an amin (trustee) who briefly violated the trust but then returned to compliance; the mudarabah contract remains in effect since the capital is still in his possession under the original contract. This ruling applies specifically when the capital owner imposed the restriction at the time of the contract.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): A usurper; one who takes property without right. Here, the mudarib who acts beyond permission is treated as a ghasib, making him liable for the capital.
أمين (amin): Trustee; the mudarib is initially an amin (trustee) over the capital. If he violates conditions, he becomes a ghasib, but returning without transaction restores his trusteership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib exceeds the specified location and buys goods there, what happens to the goods and profit?
A) The goods belong to the capital owner, and profit is shared.
B) The goods belong to the mudarib, and he gets the profit.
C) The goods are given in charity.
D) The goods are destroyed and both parties lose.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib who violates contract conditions becomes a usurper: he owns the profit but bears loss, and returns to trust if he reconsiders before transacting.

Post-Contract Restrictions and Liability in Mudarabah
Ruling on specification validity and intra-Hanafi liability dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid post-contract specification in mudarabah and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on when liability attaches for unauthorized removal of capital.

Core Idea
Owner can only add restrictions if capital is liquid; liability arises upon removal but is confirmed by purchase.

The mudarabah contract grants the mudarib broad authority. Can the capital owner later impose restrictions? This lesson examines when such specification is valid and also explores a related dispute among Hanafi authorities: if the owner specifies a location and the mudarib takes the capital elsewhere, when does he become liable? The answers hinge on the form of the capital and the timing of purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct issues: (1) validity of post-contract specification depending on capital being liquid (nadan) vs. goods ('ard), and (2) the intra-Hanafi dispute on when liability (daman) attaches if the mudarib takes capital to an unauthorized city.
The passage moves from specification to liability.
2
Analyze the ruling on specification
When the owner adds restrictions after the contract, the ruling depends on the capital's state. If the capital is liquid (cash), specification is valid because the owner can terminate the contract. If the capital is goods, specification is invalid because the owner cannot even remove the mudarib then.
Key distinction: liquid vs. goods.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Two narrations: al-Kitab says liability upon taking out; al-Jami' al-Saghir says liability only upon purchase. Al-Hidayah reconciles: liability is obligated by the taking out but is provisional; purchase confirms and stabilizes it.
The dispute is about the moment of confirmation, not obligation.
4
Apply the reconciliation
Understand the practical consequence: if the mudarib returns the capital before buying, liability is removed (he is not liable). If he buys, liability becomes definitive. This is the mufta bihi position.
This reconciles the two narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت المضاربة مطلقةً فخصها ربُّ المال بعد عقد المضاربة، نحو إن قال له: لا تبع بالنسيئة، ولا تشتر دقيقاً ولا طعاماً، أو لا تشتر من فلان، أو لا تسافر، فإن كان قبل أن يعمل المضارب أو بعدما عمل، فاشترى وباع وقبض المال فصارَ المالُ ناضاً جاز تخصيصه ؛ لأنه في هذه الحالة يملك عزله أو إخراجه عن المضاربة، فيصح تخصيصه. وإن كان التخصيص بعدما عمل فصار المالُ عرضاً لا يصح تخصيصه ؛ لأنه لو نهاه عن البيع في هذه الحالة أو أرادَ عزله لا يصح ، فلا يصح تخصيصه. كذا في «المنح». واعلم أنَّ في رواية «الكتاب»: يضمن المضارب بنفس الإخراج، وهو مطابق لما في أكثر المتون، وفي رواية «الجامع الصغير»: إنّما يضمن إذا اشترى بعد الإخراج، كما اختاره صاحب «المنح». وقال في «الهداية» : والصحيح أنَّ بالشراء يتقرر الضمان ؛ لزوال احتمال الرد إلى المصر الذي عينه، أما الضمان فوجوبه بنفس الإخراج، وإنما شرط الشراء للتقرر لا لأصل الوجوب. انتهى. وحاصله : إنَّ الضمان يجب بنفس الإخراج لكنه على شرف الزوال، فإذا اشترى به تقرر وتأكد فصار المضارب بنفس الإخراج متعدّياً ضامناً، لكنه لا يتقرر إلا بالشراء في بلد آخر، فإذا رجع قبل تقرّره زال الضمان، فبقي المال على ما كان.
And if the mudarabah was absolute, then the capital owner restricts it after the contract, like saying: Do not sell on credit, do not buy flour or food, do not buy from so-and-so, or do not travel. If it is before the mudarib acts or after he acted, bought and sold and took the cash and the capital became liquid (nadan), his specification is valid because in this situation he has the power to terminate or remove him from the mudarabah, so his specification is valid. But if the specification was after he acted and the capital became goods ('ard), his specification is not valid because if he were to forbid him from selling in this situation or wanted to remove him, it would not be valid, so his specification is not valid. This is in al-Manh. And know that in the narration of al-Kitab, the mudarib is liable by the mere act of taking out (the capital), which matches most texts. In the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, he is only liable if he buys after taking out, as chosen by the author of al-Manh. And said in al-Hidayah: The correct view is that by purchase the liability is confirmed, because the possibility of returning to the specified city is removed. As for liability, its obligation is by the mere taking out, but purchase is required for confirmation, not for the origin of obligation. End. Its summary: Liability is obligated by the mere taking out but is on the verge of removal. If he then buys with it, it becomes confirmed and established. Thus the mudarib becomes by the mere taking out a transgressor and liable, but it is not confirmed except by purchase in another city. If he returns before confirmation, liability is removed, and the capital remains as before.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage addresses when the capital owner can add specific restrictions after the mudarabah contract has already been made. The key distinction is the state of the capital: if the capital is still liquid (naqd, i.e., cash) either before the mudarib has begun trading or after he has traded and returned to cash, the owner's specification is valid. He has the authority to modify or terminate the mudarabah at that point. However, if the mudarib has already traded and the capital is now in goods ('urud), the owner cannot impose new restrictions because he cannot even remove the mudarib at that stage. The passage then discusses a related issue: if the owner specifies a location and the mudarib takes the capital out to a different city. There are two narrations: according to al-Kitab (a core text), liability arises immediately upon taking the capital out. According to al-Jami' al-Saghir, liability only arises if he actually buys something after the unauthorized removal. Al-Hidayah reconciles: liability is obligated by the mere act of taking out, but it is provisional—it is on the verge of removal if he returns without buying. The purchase confirms and stabilizes the liability. So the mudarib is a transgressor and liable from the moment he takes the capital out, but the liability is only definitively fixed if he purchases in the unauthorized location.

Key Terms
ناض (nadan): Liquid capital; cash that is ready for trade, as opposed to goods ('ard).
عرض ('ard): Goods; non-liquid assets including commodities and trade items.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.
تقرر (taqarrur): Confirmation; the point at which a fragile obligation becomes definitive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in al-Hidayah, when does the mudarib's liability (daman) become definitively confirmed for taking capital to an unauthorized location?
A) Immediately upon taking the capital out of the specified city.
B) Only when the mudarib makes a profit.
C) When the mudarib makes a purchase in the unauthorized city.
D) When the mudarib returns the capital.

Key Takeaway
Post-contract specification in mudarabah is valid only when the capital is liquid; for unauthorized location, liability is immediate but fragile until purchase.

Mudarib Cannot Marry Slaves from Mudarabah Capital
Ruling on mudarib marrying slaves from partnership funds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on a mudarib marrying slaves from mudarabah capital and evaluate Abu Yusuf's dissenting opinion.

Core Idea
A mudarib cannot marry a slave from partnership capital because marriage is not a trading activity.

This lesson examines a specific disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding the scope of a mudarib's authority. While the mudarib can buy and sell goods, can he also arrange marriages for slaves owned by the partnership? The passage contrasts the majority view with Abu Yusuf's more permissive stance, focusing on the purposes of the mudarabah contract.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
The base text (matn) states that the mudarib cannot marry a male or female slave from the mudarabah capital. This is the starting point.
Note that the ruling applies to both genders.
2
Identify the dissenting opinion
Imam Abu Yusuf permits marrying only the female slave (amah) because of two benefits: the dowry (mahr) received from the husband and the release from maintenance (nafaqah).
Abu Yusuf's view is an exception, not the norm.
3
Understand the majority's counter-argument
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that marriage is not from 'the works of trade' (أعمال التجارة). The mudarabah contract only authorizes trade-related activities, not personal or matrimonial matters.
The term 'works of trade' limits the mudarib's authority.
4
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the mudarib marries a female slave according to Abu Yusuf, the dowry becomes part of the mudarabah capital. According to the majority, such an act is a breach of trust and the mudarib may be liable.
The difference affects the distribution of profits and liability.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view: the mudarib cannot marry any slave from the mudarabah capital. This is the safer, established ruling.
Fatwa is given according to the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أن يزوج عبداً، أو أمةً من مالها : أي من مال المضاربة. وليس للمضارب أن يزوج عبداً أو أمة من مال المضاربة، وعن أبي يوسف له أن يزوج الأمة ؛ لأنه يستفيد بتزويجها المهر، وسقوط النفقة، ولهما : إن التزويج ليس من أعمال التجارة، وعقد المضاربة لا يتضمن
Nor that he marry a male slave or a female slave from her wealth: i.e., from the mudarabah capital. And the mudarib does not have the right to marry a male slave or a female slave from the mudarabah capital. And from Abu Yusuf: He has the right to marry the female slave, because he benefits by her marriage through the dowry and the removal of maintenance. And the two (Abu Yusuf's opponents) [argue]: Marriage is not from the works of trade, and the mudarabah contract does not include [such authority].
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses whether the mudarib has the authority to marry slaves that are part of the mudarabah capital. The main ruling (from the base text) is that he cannot marry any slave from the mudarabah funds. However, a view from Imam Abu Yusuf is reported: he permits marrying the female slave (the maid) because doing so brings benefits to the mudarabah: the mudarib receives the dowry (mahr) from the husband and is relieved from the obligation of providing maintenance (nafaqah) for the slave. The two (presumably Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) hold that such acts are not within the scope of trade activities, and the mudarabah contract does not authorize them. The argument is that the primary purpose of mudarabah is profit through trade, and marriage of slaves is not a trading activity. The passage ends abruptly here, but the disagreement is clear.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): Female slave; a female owned by the mudarabah partnership.
مهر (mahr): Dowry; the payment given to the bride (here the female slave's owner) upon marriage.
أعمال التجارة (a'mal al-tijarah): Works of trade; the commercial activities authorized under the mudarabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, for both male and female slaves.
B) Yes, only for female slaves because of the benefits.
C) No, for either male or female slaves.
D) Yes, but only if the capital owner agrees.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's authority under mudarabah is limited to trade; marrying slaves is not a trade activity and is therefore prohibited by the majority Hanafi view.

Mudarib Marrying Slaves from Capital
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on marrying slaves in mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits the mudarib from marrying any slave from mudarabah capital and evaluate Abu Yusuf's dissenting view.

Core Idea
The mudarib's authority is limited to trade; marriage is not trade, so marrying slaves from capital is prohibited.

This passage addresses a specific boundary of the mudarib's authority: can he use mudarabah capital to marry a slave? While some might see financial benefit, the Sahibayn firmly rule that marriage is not a trade activity and thus outside the contract. We'll examine the reasoning and the practical consequence of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi ruling
Identify the matn's claim: the mudarib may not marry a male or female slave from the mudarabah capital.
This is the base ruling in the text.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's exception
Note that Abu Yusuf permits marrying the slave-girl because of the benefit (dowry and removal of maintenance).
He sees a financial advantage analogous to trade.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's rebuttal
Both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) argue that marriage is not a trade activity, and the mudarabah contract only authorizes trade.
They analogize to kitabah and freeing for money: acquisition but not trade.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the mudarib marries a slave-girl, the marriage is valid but he is personally liable for the dowry, not the mudarabah capital.
This shows the dispute does not nullify the marriage but affects liability.
5
Establish the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is prohibition for both male and female slaves, following the Sahibayn's reasoning.
This is the view adopted for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أن يزوج عبداً، أو أمةً من مالها. قوله : ولا أن يزوج ...الخ ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يزوج عبداً أو أمة من مال المضاربة، وعن أبي يوسف له أن يزوج الأمة ؛ لأنه يستفيد بتزويجها المهر، وسقوط النفقة، ولهما : إن التزويج ليس من أعمال التجارة، وعقد المضاربة لا يتضمن إلا التوكيل بأعمال التجارة، فصار كالكتابة والعتق على مال، فإنّهما اكتساب، ولكن لم يكونا من أعمال التجارة لم يدخلا في عقد المضاربة.
And he should not marry a male slave or a female slave from its capital. He said: 'And he should not marry...' meaning it is not permissible for the mudarib to marry a male slave or a female slave from the mudarabah capital. From Abu Yusuf: he may marry the female slave because he benefits from the dowry and the removal of maintenance. And from both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): Marriage is not among trade activities, and the mudarabah contract only includes agency in trade activities, so it is like kitabah and freeing for money; they are acquisition but not trade activities, so they are not included in the mudarabah contract.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states that the mudarib (agent in a mudarabah) cannot marry a slave (male or female) from the mudarabah capital. The commentator explains that this is because marriage is not a trade activity. He then reports a difference: Abu Yusuf permits marrying the slave-girl because the mudarib benefits from the dowry and the removal of maintenance expenses. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that marriage is not a trade activity, and the mudarabah contract only includes agency in trade, not marriage. Therefore, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that the mudarib cannot marry slaves from the mudarabah capital.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership wherein one partner provides capital and the other provides labor for trade.
مضارب (mudarib): The managing partner who provides labor in a mudarabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the mudarib prohibited from marrying a slave from the mudarabah capital?
A) Marriage is not a trade activity and the mudarabah only authorizes trade.
B) The dowry is not considered profit.
C) Abu Yusuf's opinion is the relied-upon one.
D) Marriage requires a separate contract.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's authority is limited to trade; marriage is not trade, so marrying slaves from capital is prohibited.

Mudarib's Prohibition on Purchasing a Slave Freed by Rabb al-Mal
Ruling with talil and consequence for prohibited purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mudarib purchases a slave who would be freed upon the rabb al-mal and explain the reason (talil) and legal consequence.

Core Idea
A mudarib cannot purchase a slave who becomes free upon the rabb al-mal because it prevents repeated profit transactions.

In mudarabah, the mudarib is entrusted with capital to trade for profit. Certain purchases defeat this purpose, such as buying a slave who automatically becomes free upon purchase by the capital owner. This lesson explores the ruling, its rationale, and what happens if the mudarib violates it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mudarib purchasing a slave who would be freed upon the rabb al-mal. The ruling is that it is prohibited (haram).
2
Understand the reason (talil)
Mudarabah is legislated for profit through repeated transactions. Purchasing a slave who immediately becomes free ends the possibility of selling or trading further, thus defeating the purpose.
The reason is rooted in the maqṣid (objective) of the contract.
3
Contrast with agency (wakala)
An absolute agent may purchase such a slave because agency is not inherently restricted to profit-oriented trade. However, if the principal restricts the agency (e.g., 'buy for resale'), it becomes similar to mudarabah.
This contrast clarifies the scope of mudarabah's restriction.
4
Determine the consequence of violation
If the mudarib makes such a purchase, it is for his own account (not the mudarabah), and he is liable to the rabb al-mal for the price paid from the capital.
The purchase is valid but the mudarib bears the loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أن يشتري مَن يُعْتَقَ على ربِّ المال فلو شَرَى كان له لا لها. قوله : ولا أن يشتري ...الخ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يشتري مــن يعتق بالشراء على ربِّ المال، سواء كان ذلك العتق لقرابة بأن كان العبد المشترى ذا رحم مُحَرَّم منه كأبيه وابنه، أو ليمين بأن قال رب المال : إن اشتريت فلاناً فهو حرّ، والمضارب اشتراه. ووجهه : إن عقد المضاربة إنما شرع لتحصيل الربح، وهو لا يكون إلا بالتصرف مرة بعد أخرى ، وذا لا يتحقق فيمن يعتق على ربِّ المال، بخلاف الوكيل بشراء العبد، حيث يجوز له أن يشتري من يعتق على الموكل؛ لأنَّ التوكيل مطلق، فيجري على إطلاقه. وهاهنا مقيد بمال يمكن التجارة فيه، حتى لو وجد في الوكالة أيضاً ما يدل على التقييد، بأن قال: اشتر لي عبداً أبيعه أو استخدمه، أو جارية أطؤها كان الحكم كذلك. قوله : فلو شرى ... الخ؛ يعني المضارب لو اشترى من يعتق على ربِّ المال صار مشترياً لنفسه، وضمن لرب المال؛ لأنه نقد الثمن من مال المضاربة، وعندما مالك لو كان عالماً موسراً ضمن ، وإلا لا، ذكره العَيْنِي، وغيره.
And he should not purchase someone who would be freed upon the rabb al-mal. If he purchases, it is for himself, not for it. He said: 'And he should not purchase...' meaning it is not permissible for the mudarib to purchase someone who is freed by purchase upon the rabb al-mal, whether that emancipation is due to kinship (the purchased slave being a mahram relative like father or son) or due to an oath (the rabb al-mal said: 'If you buy so-and-so, he is free') and the mudarib buys him. The reason is that the mudarabah contract was legislated only to obtain profit, and that is only through repeated transactions, and this is not achieved regarding one who is freed upon the rabb al-mal, unlike the agent in buying a slave, where he may purchase someone who is freed upon the principal, because agency is absolute, so it operates on its absolute scope. Here it is restricted to capital that can be traded, so even in agency if there is an indication of restriction, such as if he said, 'Buy me a slave to sell or use, or a slave-girl to have intercourse with,' the ruling is the same. He said: 'If he purchases...' meaning if the mudarib purchases someone who would be freed upon the rabb al-mal, he becomes a purchaser for himself and is liable to the rabb al-mal because he paid the price from mudarabah capital. According to Malik, if he was aware and solvent, he is liable, otherwise not; 'Aynee and others mentioned this.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling that the mudarib cannot purchase a slave who, if purchased, would become free due to the rabb al-mal's relationship (e.g., father, son) or due to an oath. The reason is that such a purchase cannot be a repeated transaction for profit, which is the purpose of mudarabah. Unlike a general agent who may purchase such a slave, the mudarib is restricted to trade. If the mudarib violates this, the purchase is for his own account, and he is liable for the price paid from the mudarabah capital. The commentary also notes a Maliki position on liability conditions.

Key Terms
يعتق على رب المال (yu'taqu 'ala rabb al-mal): A slave who becomes free by virtue of being purchased by the capital owner due to kinship or oath.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital provider in a mudarabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It prevents the mudarabah from achieving profit through repeated transactions.
C) It reduces the capital available for trade.
D) It violates the rule against selling what one does not own.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib is prohibited from buying a slave who becomes free upon the rabb al-mal, and if he does, the purchase is for himself and he must compensate the capital.

Prohibition on Purchasing a Relative in Mudarabah
Brief ruling on mudarabah prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the mudarib purchasing a slave who would be freed upon him and identify the condition (profit) that triggers the prohibition.

Core Idea
A mudarib cannot purchase a relative slave when there is profit, as it would end the partnership.

This lesson covers a specific restriction in mudarabah: the mudarib is prohibited from buying a slave who would automatically become free upon him due to kinship. The commentary explains that this applies only if the mudarabah has profit, and the reason is that the slave's freedom would disrupt the ongoing trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the mudarib purchasing a slave who is a relative (e.g., father, son) who would be freed upon the mudarib. The ruling is that it is prohibited (not permissible).
This applies only to slaves who would be freed automatically due to kinship, not to any slave.
2
Identify the condition of profit
The prohibition is conditional on the presence of profit in the mudarabah. If there is no profit, the ruling may differ.
The text says 'if there is profit', meaning the mudarib cannot use profit funds to buy such a relative.
3
Understand the rationale
Buying a relative slave leads to his automatic freedom, which removes capital from the mudarabah and ends the partnership. This harms the rabb al-mal's interest.
This is similar to the previous ruling where the mudarib cannot buy a slave who is already free.
4
Confirm the expansion
The commentary expands the ruling to clarify that this prohibition is an extension of the earlier rule about buying free persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا من يعتق عليه إن كان ربح. قوله : ولا من يعتق عليه...الخ؛ يعني وليس للمضارب أن يشتري من يعتق عليه.
And nor someone who would be freed upon him if there is profit. He said: 'And nor someone who would be freed upon him...' meaning it is not permissible for the mudarib to purchase someone who would be freed upon him.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This is a short additional ruling: the mudarib cannot purchase a slave who would be freed upon himself (i.e., his own relative) if there is profit in the mudarabah. The purchase would result in the slave's freedom, which prevents ongoing trade, similar to the previous ruling. The commentary only explains the meaning without further elaboration.

Key Terms
من يعتق عليه (man yu'taqu 'alayh): A slave who would become free upon the mudarib due to kinship (e.g., father, son).
مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a mudarabah contract who provides labor and management.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital provider in a mudarabah contract.

Key Takeaway

Mudarib Buying a Relative Slave: Liability and Emancipation
Expansion of matn on liability and emancipation in mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mudarib becomes liable when buying a relative slave in a mudarabah, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the divisibility of emancipation.

Core Idea
A mudarib is liable only if the slave's value exceeds the capital at purchase; later increase does not create liability.

When a mudarib (working partner) buys a slave who would be freed upon him (e.g., his father or son), the ruling depends on the slave's value relative to the mudarabah capital. If the value exceeds the capital, the mudarib is deemed to have bought the slave for himself and is personally liable. If the value is equal or less, the purchase is valid for the partnership. A subsequent increase in value frees the mudarib's share without liability, but triggers a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over whether the capital owner's share also becomes free.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mudarib purchasing a slave who is a relative (e.g., son) using mudarabah funds. Determine the ruling based on the slave's value at the time of purchase compared to the capital.
The ruling changes if the value is more than the capital (liability), equal/less (valid), or increases later (partial freedom).
2
Analyse the condition for liability
Liability (daman) applies if the slave's value exceeds the capital at purchase. The mudarib is deemed to have bought for himself, so he must compensate the capital owner.
The text: 'ولو فعل ضَمِن' and 'قيمته أكثر من رأس المال ضمن المضارب'.
3
Examine the scenario of no profit
If there is no profit (value ≤ capital), the mudarib does not own the slave (it is encumbered by capital), so the purchase is valid for mudarabah and no emancipation occurs.
No liability, no freedom.
4
Understand the increase in value after purchase
If the value increases later (e.g., from 1000 to 1500), the mudarib's share becomes free proportionally, but he is not liable because he had no role in the increase. This is analogized to inheritance.
The analogy: a woman buys her husband's son, then dies; the husband's share becomes free without liability to the brother.
5
Differentiate the intra-Hanafi dispute on tajazzu' al-i'taq
According to Abu Hanifa, emancipation is indivisible, so the capital owner's share becomes corrupted (fasid) – the slave is neither fully free nor fully owned. According to the Sahibayn, emancipation is divisible, so the entire slave becomes free.
Practical consequence: wala' (patronage) is shared under Abu Hanifa, but belongs entirely to the mudarib under the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو فعل ضَمِن ، وإن لم يكن ربح صَحَ ، فإن زادت قيمته عتق حصته ، ولم يَضمَنْ شيئا) ؛ لأنه لا صنع له في زيادة القيمة عليه ذلك للمضاربة إن كان في المالِ ربح ؛ لأنه يعتق نصيبه ويفسد بسببه نصيب ربِّ المال ؛ لانتفاء جواز بيعه ؛ لكونه مستسعى لا يجوز بيعه، أو يفسد نصيب رب المال عند أبي حنيفة له، ويعتق عندهما ، بناء على تجزؤ الإعتاق وعدمه. والمراد من الربح : أن يكون قيمة العبدِ المشترى أكثر من رأس المال، سواء كان في جملة رأس المال ربح أو لا ؛ لأنه إذا كان قيمة العين مثل رأس المال أو أقل لا يظهرُ ملك المضارب فيه، بل يجعل مشغولاً برأس المال حتى إذا كان رأس المال ألفاً، وصار عشرة آلاف. ثم اشترى المضاربُ مَن يعتق عليه، وقيمته ألف أو أقل لا يعتق عليه، وكذا لو كان له ثلاثة أولاد أو أكثر، وقيمةُ كلُّ واحدٍ ألف أو أقل، فاشتراهم لا يعتق منهم واحد ؛ لأنَّ كل واحد منهم مشغول برأس المال. ولا يملك المضارب منهم شيئاً حتى تزيد قيمة كل عين على رأس المال على حدة من غير ضمّه إلى آخر ؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يهلك منهم اثنان ، فيتعين الباقي لرأس المال ، ولعدم الأولوية (1) قوله : ولو فعل....الخ ؛ يعني لو اشترى من يعتق عليه، وقيمته أكثر من رأس المال ضمن المضارب ؛ لأنه يصير مشترياً ذلك العبد لنفسه، فيضمن بالنقد من مال المضاربة. (٢) قوله : وإن لم يكن ...الخ ؛ يعني وإن لم يكن في المال ريح صح اشتراؤه للمضاربة ؛ لأنه إذا لم يزد قيمته على رأس المال لا يعتق عليه، إذ لا ملك للمضارب فيه ؛ لكونه مشغولاً برأس المال، فيمكنه أن يبيعه للمضاربة فيصح. [٣]قوله : فإن زادت...الخ ؛ توضيحه أنه إذا ظهر ريحُ في المشترى بعد الشراء بأن كان رأس المال ألف درهم، وكانت قيمة ذلك العبد المشترى وقت الشراء كذلك أو أقل ثم زادت قيمته على رأس المال بأن صارت ألفاً وخمسمئة عتق نصيب المضارب منه ، لأنه ملك بعض قريبه. فوجب أن يعتق بقدره، ولم يضمن لرب المالِ شيئاً من قيمته ؛ لأنه لا صنع في زيادة القيمة، وإنّما أعتق عليه بطريق الحكم بسبب زيادة القيمة من غير اختياره ، فصار كما إذا ورثه مع غيره ؛ بأن اشترتِ امرأة ابن زوجها ثم ماتت وتركت هذا الزوج وأخاً، عتق نصيب الزوج، ولا يضمن شيئاً لأخيها. [1] قوله : وسعى العبد... الخ ؛ لأنه احتبست مالية العبد عند العبد فيسعى فيه، واعلم أنّ في هذه الصورة كان ولاء ذلك العبد بينهما عند الإمام، وعندهما: كله وسعى العبد في رأس المال وحصة ربِّ المال من الربح، وهذا بناء على اختلاف تجزؤ الربح وعدمه.
And if he does so [i.e., buys a relative], he is liable; and if there is no profit, the purchase is valid; then if its value increases, his share becomes free, and he is not liable for anything because he has no role in the increase in value. That [ruling] applies to mudarabah if there is profit in the capital because his share becomes free and because of that the capital owner's share is corrupted due to the impossibility of selling it, because it is mustas'a and cannot be sold; or the capital owner's share is corrupted according to Abu Hanifa, and it becomes free according to the two companions, based on the divisibility of emancipation and its indivisibility. The meaning of profit is that the value of the purchased slave is more than the capital, whether there is profit in the total capital or not; because if the value of the item is equal to the capital or less, the mudarib's ownership does not manifest; rather it is considered encumbered by the capital. So if the capital is one thousand and becomes ten thousand, then the mudarib buys someone who would be freed upon him, and his value is one thousand or less, he does not become free; similarly, if he has three children or more, and the value of each is one thousand or less, and he buys them, not one of them becomes free because each is encumbered by the capital. And the mudarib does not own any of them until the value of each item exceeds the capital separately without combining with another; because it is possible that two of them perish, so the remaining one is allocated to the capital, and due to lack of precedence. (1) His statement 'and if he does so...' etc.: meaning if he buys someone who would be freed upon him, and his value is greater than the capital, the mudarib is liable because he becomes the buyer of that slave for himself, so he is liable with the cash from the mudarabah property. (2) His statement 'and if there is no profit...' etc.: meaning if there is no profit in the capital, his purchase for the mudarabah is valid; because if its value does not exceed the capital, he does not become free, as he has no ownership in it because it is encumbered by the capital, so he can sell it for the mudarabah, so it is valid. [3] His statement 'then if its value increases...' etc.: Explanation: if profit appears in the purchased item after purchase, as when the capital is one thousand dirhams and the value of that purchased slave at the time of purchase was likewise or less, then its value increased above the capital, becoming one thousand five hundred: the mudarib's share becomes free, because he owns part of his relative. So it must become free proportionally, and he is not liable to the capital owner for anything of its value because he has no role in the increase in value; rather it is freed upon him by way of legal ruling due to the increase in value without his choice, so it is like the case when he inherits him with others: if a woman buys the son of her husband, then she dies leaving this husband and a brother, the husband's share becomes free, and he is not liable to her brother for anything. [1] His statement 'and the slave works for the value of his share...' etc.: because the value of the slave is retained with the slave, so he works for it. Know that in this case the wala' of that slave is shared between them according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa], and according to the two companions: all of it [for the mudarib] and the slave works for the capital and the capital owner's share of the profit; this is based on the difference [of opinion] regarding the divisibility of profit and its indivisibility.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling concerning a mudarib who buys a slave who is his relative (e.g., son or father) and thus would be set free upon ownership. The author explains that the ruling depends on two factors: whether there is profit in the mudarabah capital at the time of purchase, and whether the slave's value exceeds the capital. If the slave's value at purchase is more than the capital, the mudarib is personally liable because he is deemed to have bought the slave for himself, not for the mudarabah. If there is no profit and the value equals or is less than the capital, the purchase is valid for the mudarabah because the mudarib does not actually own the slave (it is encumbered by the capital). If the slave's value increases after purchase (e.g., from one thousand to one thousand five hundred), the mudarib's share becomes free without liability, as he had no hand in the increase. The author also notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, emancipation is indivisible, so the capital owner's share is corrupted (i.e., the slave is not freed for him), while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, emancipation is divisible, so the entire slave becomes free. The example of a woman buying her husband's son and then dying is used to illustrate the principle of inheritance by analogy. Finally, the author mentions that in this scenario, the wala' (patronage) of the slave is shared between the mudarib and capital owner according to Abu Hanifa, but belongs entirely to the mudarib according to the two companions.

Key Terms
تجزؤ الإعتاق (tajazzu' al-i'taq): The question of whether emancipation can be partial (divisible) or must be complete (indivisible).
ربح (ribh): Profit in mudarabah; excess of the slave's value over the capital.
مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a mudarabah contract.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital owner/investor in a mudarabah.
مستسعى (mustas'a): A slave who is allowed to work to pay off a debt or value; here, one whose ownership is disputed cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the slave's value is equal to the capital
B) When the slave's value exceeds the capital at the time of purchase
C) When the slave's value increases after purchase
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's liability and emancipation rights depend on the slave's value relative to capital at purchase and later; the divisibility of emancipation creates an intra-Hanafi dispute affecting the capital owner's share and wala'.
